The Definition of Knowledge as 'Tabyīn' and Its Critique
Definition of knowledge as 'tabyīn' critiqued for three problems.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the definition of knowledge as 'clarification of the known' is problematic, especially for divine knowledge.

Core Idea
The word 'clarification' implies prior hiddenness, incompatible with God's eternal knowledge.

We now examine a specific definition of knowledge: 'clarification (tabyīn) of the known as it is.' At first glance, this seems plausible, but scholars noticed it carries hidden assumptions. This short passage unpacks those assumptions, showing why the definition fails not only for general reasons but specifically for God's knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's critique come through.
Notice how the passage moves from the definition to the critique to an explanation of the word pattern.
2
Identify the three critiques
The passage mentions three problems: redundancy, circularity, and that 'clarification' implies manifestation after concealment. Find these in the text.
3
Understand the word 'tabyīn'
The commentary explains that 'tabyīn' is on the pattern of tafʿīl, describing the knower. Reflect on why this pattern matters.
The pattern tafaʿʿul would describe the known, but that is not used here.
4
Connect to God's knowledge
The critique that 'clarification implies prior hiddenness' excludes God's knowledge. Ask yourself: Why is this a fatal flaw for a definition of knowledge intended to cover divine knowledge?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهم عبارات قريبة من هذا العبارات المذكورة نحو تبيين المعلوم على ما هو به، وفيه الزيادة المذكورة والدور وأن التبيين مشعر بالظهور بعد الخفاء، فيخرج عنه علمه تعالى
قوله: (تبيين المعلوم على صيغة التفعيل ليكون صفة للعالم فيصح حمله على العلم معني لأ على صيغة التفعل فإنه صيغة المعلوم، فكأنه قيل: تمييز المعلوم وكشفه على ما هو به.
قوله: ( وأن التبيين مشعر إلخ لأنه مشتق من البينونة وهو الفصل بين الشيئين بعد الاتصال فكان الشيء قبل العلم به كان مشتبهاً بأمثاله عند العالم فإذا علمه فصله عنها وأظهره.
And they have expressions close to these mentioned expressions, such as: clarification (tabyīn) of the known as it is. In it is the mentioned redundancy, circularity, and that clarification implies manifestation after concealment, so God's knowledge is excluded from it. His statement: 'clarification of the known on the pattern of tafʿīl so that it is a description of the knower, so it is valid to apply it to knowledge, meaning? لا on the pattern of tafaʿʿul, for it is the pattern of the known. So it is as if it were said: distinguishing the known and unveiling it as it is.' His statement: 'and that clarification implies etc.' because it is derived from al-baynūna, which is separation between two things after connection. So the thing before knowledge of it was similar to its likes in the mind of the knower; when he knows it, he separates it from them and reveals it.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage handles a definition of knowledge as 'clarification (tabyīn) of the known as it is.' The critique points out three problems: redundancy (the qualifier 'as it is'), circularity, and that 'clarification' implies the known was hidden, which does not apply to God's knowledge. The commentary explains that the word 'tabyīn' is on the pattern of tafʿīl, making it a description of the knower, while if on the pattern of tafaʿʿul it would describe the known. The definition would then mean 'distinguishing and unveiling the known as it is.' The comment on 'clarification implies' roots it in the concept of separation (baynūna), suggesting the known was previously mixed with others in the mind. Thus, this definition is unsuitable for divine knowledge, which has no prior ignorance.

Key Terms
تبيين
الخفاء
البينونة
التمييز
الكشف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'clarification' is a neutral term that simply means 'making clear,' without any additional implications.
Correction: The passage shows that 'tabyīn' is derived from 'baynūna' (separation between two things after connection), so it necessarily implies that the known was previously hidden or mixed with other things. This makes the definition unsuitable for God's knowledge, which is eternal and without prior hiddenness.

Checkpoint

B) Because clarification implies the known was hidden before
C) Because God's knowledge is not acquired
D) Because the definition is circular

Key Takeaway
The definition of knowledge as 'clarification of the known' fails because 'clarification' inherently implies a prior state of hiddenness, making it incompatible with God's eternal knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The passage shows that even a seemingly neutral word like 'clarification' carries assumptions. In your daily life, do you reflect on the meanings of the words you use when speaking about Allah? What might this reveal about your reverence?
?

Why 'Affirmation of the Known' Fails as a Definition
Critique of the definition 'affirmation of the known'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four main criticisms against defining knowledge as 'affirmation of the known' and why the 'maker' objection is especially decisive.

Core Idea
Defining knowledge as 'affirmation' would make us makers of what we know—impossible for God.

Sanusi now turns to another proposed definition of knowledge: 'affirmation (ithbāt) of the known as it is.' While it might seem intuitive, he uncovers serious flaws—including redundancy, circularity, and the implication that knowing God would require 'making' Him exist. This lesson unpacks each critique and shows why this definition is rejected.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how Sanusi lists multiple objections in quick succession.
The passage is dense; focus on the logical flow of the objections.
2
Identify the four objections
The passage mentions redundancy, circularity, the 'maker' problem, and ambiguity of the term 'ithbāt'. List them and see how each undermines the definition.
The 'maker' objection is the most serious and is elaborated in the commentary.
3
Focus on the 'maker' objection
Read the commentary on why knowledge of God would make the knower a 'maker' of His existence. Why is this impossible?
Even if it's mental affirmation, it still implies making, which is inappropriate for God.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why a correct definition of knowledge must avoid such implications. How does this critique prepare you for the next definition?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو إثباته أي إثبات المعلوم على ما هو به وفيه الزيادة والدور وأنه يلزم أن يكون العالم بوجوده تعالى مثبتاً له، وهو محال وأيضاً الإثبات يطلق على الإيجاد وعلى تسكين الشيء عن الحركة ولا مجال ها هنا لإرادة شيء منهما، وقد يطلق علم العلم تجوزاً، فيلزم تعريف الشيء بنفسه
قوله: يلزم أن يكون إلخ) يعني أن الإثبات هو جعل الشيء ثابتاً بأي معنى يفسر الثبوت فالعالم منا بوجوده تعالى في الخارج مثلاً يكون جاعلاً لوجوده ثابتاً، وهو محال لأن ذاته ليس محلاً للجعل، وإنما خص الوجود بالذكر، لأنه أبين استحالة، ومن هذا ظهر وجه تخصيص الاعتراض بعلمنا بالباري تعالى واندفاع ما قيل : لا استحالة في كون العلم بوجوده إثبات الوجود له في الذهن وإنه لا تتوقف الاستحالة المذكورة على تفسير الإثبات ولذا قدمه على التفسير.
قوله: (مثبتاً له وهو محال) قيل : لا استحالة في كون العلم بوجوده إثبات الوجود له في الذهن، ولا يلزم أن لا يكون له وجود سوى الوجود العلمي.
Or its affirmation (ithbāt), i.e., affirmation of the known as it is. In it is the redundancy, circularity, and that it would require that the knower of His existence be a maker of Him, which is impossible. Also, affirmation is used for bringing into existence and for settling a thing from movement, and there is no room here to intend either of them. And sometimes 'knowledge of knowledge' is used metaphorically, so it would entail defining a thing by itself. His statement: 'it would require etc.' means that affirmation is making the thing firm, by whatever meaning one interprets firmness. So the knower from among us of His existence in the external, for example, would be a maker of His existence firm, which is impossible because His essence is not a locus for making. Existence is specifically mentioned because it is more evidently impossible. From this the reason for specifying the objection to our knowledge of the Creator is clear, and the response that there is no impossibility in knowledge of His existence being an affirmation of existence in the mind is refuted, and that the impossibility does not depend on the interpretation of affirmation, so he presented it before the interpretation. His statement: 'a maker of Him, and it is impossible' – it was said: there is no impossibility in knowledge of His existence being an affirmation of existence in the mind, and it does not entail that He has no existence other than mental existence.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage critiques another definition of knowledge: 'affirmation (ithbāt) of the known as it is.' The critique points out: (1) redundancy and circularity; (2) it would make the knower a 'maker' of the known, which is impossible for God; (3) 'affirmation' is ambiguous (it can mean creating or fixing), neither applying here; (4) it can also be used metaphorically leading to self-definition. The commentary elaborates: affirmation means making something firm (jaʿl al-shayʾ thābitan). If we know God's existence, we would be 'making' His existence firm, impossible. The objection that this only applies to mental existence is dismissed because the impossibility is not about the mode; even if it were mental affirmation, it still is making, which is inappropriate for God.

Key Terms
إثبات
جعل
ثبوت
الوجود العلمي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that affirming God's existence in the mind is harmless and not 'making' in a problematic sense.
Correction: The passage shows that any act of 'affirmation' (ithbāt) is a form of 'making' (jaʿl), and God's existence cannot be made—even mentally—because His essence is not subject to being made firm.

Checkpoint
What is the primary impossibility claimed by the critique of defining knowledge as 'ithbāt al-maʿlūm'?
A) Circularity
B) Redundancy
C) It makes the knower a 'maker' of God
D) It is ambiguous

Key Takeaway
Defining knowledge as 'affirmation of the known' fails because it implies the knower is a 'maker' of what is known—an impossibility for knowledge of God—and is further flawed by redundancy, circularity, and ambiguity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The critique shows that using ordinary language to define knowledge of God leads to absurdities. Are there areas in your faith where you rely on comfortable definitions that might actually mislead you? What would it mean to let go of those definitions and rely solely on revelation?

Why the Definition of Knowledge as Trust (al-Thiqa) Is Rejected
The author critiques the definition of knowledge as trust, arguing it implies God's reliance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the definition of knowledge as trust (al-thiqa) is rejected in Islamic theology, particularly because it would imply that God relies on His own knowledge, which contradicts divine perfection.

Core Idea
The definition of knowledge as trust fails because trust implies need, making it inapplicable to God.

In this passage, the author discusses a definition of knowledge as 'trust' or 'reliance' upon the known reality. While the definition has some logical issues like circularity, the strongest objection is theological: applying this definition to God would imply that He is dependent on His own knowledge, which is incompatible with His absolute independence. Let's examine the arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and its literal translation to get the author's full argument.
Notice the concise style of the passage.
2
Identify the three criticisms mentioned
The passage points out redundancy, circularity, and the theological problem. Underline each in the text.
The theological problem is the main focus.
3
Focus on the theological critique
Understand why the definition would make God 'wāthiqa' (trustful) and why that is impermissible.
Trust implies reliance on something external.
4
Examine the lexical evidence
The commentary explains that 'thiqa' indicates incapacity and weakness, supported by hadith and dictionary entries.
This confirms that trust is not a divine attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو الثقة بأنه ( أي المعلوم ) على ما هو به ) وفيه الزيادة والدور، وإنه يوجب كون الباري تعالى واثقاً بما هو عالم به، وذلك مما يمتنع إطلاقه عليه شرعاً
قوله: ( وإنه يوجب إلخ) يعني أنه تعريف للعلم المطلق فيكون شاملاً لعلمه تعالى فيوجب كونه تعالى واثقاً بما علمه .
قوله : ( وذلك إلخ) أي كون الباري واثقاً بما علمه مما يمتنع إطلاقه عليه شرعاً بأي لفظ عنه، فلا يصح إطلاق العالم لأنه دليل العجز والضعف في شمس العلوم، وثق به ثقة إذا اعتمد عليه وفي الحديث الثقة بكل أحد عجز وفي التاج الثقة والموثق استوار شدن ويعدى بالباء .
Or reliance (al-thiqa) that it (i.e., the known) is as it is. In it is the redundancy, circularity, and that it necessitates the Creator being trustful (wāthiqa) of what He knows, and that is something whose application to Him is prohibited religiously. His statement: 'and that it necessitates etc.' means that it is a definition of absolute knowledge, so it includes God's knowledge, necessitating that He is trustful of what He knows. His statement: 'and that etc.' means that the Creator being trustful of what He knows is something whose application to Him is prohibited religiously, by any word. So it is not valid to apply 'trustful' to Him, because it indicates incapacity and weakness. In Shams al-ʿUlūm: 'wathiqa bihi thiqa' when one relies on him. And in the hadith: 'trust in everyone is incapacity.' And in al-Tāj: 'al-thiqa and al-mūthiq mean trusting, and it is transitive with the preposition bā'.'
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage examines the definition of knowledge as 'trust/reliance (al-thiqa) that the known is as it is.' The three standard criticisms (redundancy, circularity) are mentioned, but the main point is that this definition would attribute trust (thiqa) to God, implying reliance on His own knowledge. This is religiously impermissible (mumtaniʿ sharʿan). The commentary explains that the definition is for absolute knowledge, so it covers God's knowledge, making Him a 'trustful' being. But trust implies dependence or weakness, which is unfitting for God. The commentary cites a hadith and lexical sources to support that 'trust' implies need, which cannot be ascribed to God. Thus, this definition is rejected.

Key Terms
الثقة
الواثق
العجز
الضعف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'trust' is a neutral term that could apply to God without negative connotation.
Correction: The passage shows that 'trust' (thiqa) inherently implies need and weakness, as evidenced by language and hadith, making it unfitting for God.

Checkpoint
Why is the definition of knowledge as 'trust (al-thiqa) that the known is as it is' rejected regarding God?
A) It is circular
B) It implies God trusts Himself, which is redundant
C) Trust implies weakness or need
D) It is only valid for humans

Key Takeaway
Defining knowledge as trust fails because it implies dependency, which cannot be attributed to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever described God's knowledge as 'trustworthy' or 'reliable'? After this lesson, do you see how even well-intentioned language can imply need? Will you be more vigilant in how you speak about Allah?
?
The Prophet ﷺ said, 'Trust in everyone is incapacity.' When you trust others, do you ever feel that same sense of reliance? How does this help you understand why Allah cannot be described as 'trustful'? Will you now reflect on your own attachments?
?
This definition was rejected because it made God appear dependent. Are there any beliefs in your heart that subtly attribute need or limitation to Allah? Ask yourself honestly: Do I truly believe Allah is free of all need? What one step can you take today to purify your understanding of His perfection?

Imam al-Razi's Definition of Knowledge: Firm Belief Matching the Cause
Imam al-Razi defines knowledge as firm belief matching the cause, originating from necessity or reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's definition of knowledge and its key components: firm belief, matching the cause, and originating from necessity or acquired reasoning.

Core Idea
Knowledge is not just firm belief but belief that matches reality and arises from a valid cause.

Imam al-Razi's definition of knowledge is a landmark in Islamic epistemology. It combines subjective certainty with objective correspondence and causal origin. In this lesson, we examine the definition as presented by al-Razi, along with the beginning of the commentary that clarifies its conditions. Although the passage is incomplete, it gives us the essential structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definition and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the English translation. Notice the key terms: 'iʿtiqād jāzim' (firm belief) and 'muwāfiq li-mūjib' (matching the cause).
The Arabic uses 'اعتقاد جازم' for firm belief and 'مطابق لموجب' for matching the cause.
2
Understand the condition of 'matching the cause'
The phrase 'li-mūjib' means that the belief must correspond to what necessitates it. The commentary explains that this belief arises from either necessity (ḍarūra) or reasoned acquisition (naẓar).
Necessity refers to self-evident truths; acquisition refers to knowledge gained through reasoning.
3
Recognize the two sources of the cause
Identify the two sources mentioned: 'imā ḍarūratan aw' (either necessarily or [acquired]). This means the cause can be either a necessary (self-evident) truth or a conclusion from reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

قوله: (لموجب) أي يكون ذلك الاعتقاد المقيد بالجزم والمطابقة ناشئاً عن ضرورة، أو
قوله: ( مثبتاً له وهو محال) قيل : لا استحالة في كون العلم بوجوده إثبات الوجود له في الذهن، ولا يلزم أن لا يكون له وجود سوى الوجود العلمي .
قوله : ( وذلك مما يمتنع إطلاقه عليه تعالى شرعاً أجيب عنه بأن امتناع إطلاقه عليه تعالى
The fifth: by Imam al-Razi: that it is a firm belief (iʿtiqād jāzim) matching the cause (muwāfiq li-mūjib), either necessarily or [acquired]. His statement: 'to a cause (li-mūjib)' – meaning that this belief, qualified by firmness and matching, arises from necessity or [acquired]. His statement: 'a maker of Him, and it is impossible' – it was said: there is no impossibility in knowledge of His existence being an affirmation of existence in the mind, and it does not entail that He has no existence other than mental existence. His statement: 'and that is something whose application to Him is prohibited religiously' – it was answered that the prohibition of applying it to Him [is...]
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage begins Imam al-Razi's definition of knowledge: it is 'firm belief (iʿtiqād jāzim) that corresponds to the cause (muwāfiq li-mūjib), whether necessarily or by acquisition.' The commentary explains that 'li-mūjib' means the belief arises from a cause, either necessary (self-evident) or theoretical. The rest of the page then returns to a previous discussion (the 'ithbāt' definition), possibly as a cross-reference. The last line is a response to an earlier objection about the impossibility of applying 'trust' to God, but it cuts off. This lesson is incomplete due to the page break. The definition itself is only partially presented; the full definition typically includes 'al-ʿilm huwa al-iʿtiqād al-jāzim al-muwāfiq li-l-mūjib ʿan ḍarūra aw naẓar' (knowledge is firm belief matching the cause, from necessity or proof).

Misconception: One might think that 'firm belief' alone suffices for knowledge.
Correction: Al-Razi's definition adds the condition that the belief must match its cause, i.e., correspond to reality and arise from a valid source.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi defines knowledge as firm belief that corresponds to its cause, whether that cause is self-evident (necessary) or derived through reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What is the cause behind your belief in His existence—is it necessary (self-evident) or acquired through reasoning? If it is acquired, have you personally verified that reasoning, or are you relying on others?
?
If knowledge is defined as belief matching the cause, then your certainty about the unseen (e.g., the Hereafter) must be grounded in a cause that makes it unavoidable. What is that cause for you? Does your daily life reflect that cause, or are you living as if the cause is weak?
?
This lesson distinguishes mental existence from external reality. Is your knowledge of Allah merely a concept in your mind, or does it correspond to a living relationship with your Creator? What practical steps can you take today to ensure that your knowledge of Him is not just intellectual but transforms your actions?

Objection to Defining Knowledge as Itqan
An objection to defining knowledge as itqan and its response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection to defining knowledge as 'itqan' (perfecting) and the response that distinguishes perfecting the specific object from perfecting in general.

Core Idea
Not all knowledge is an act, so defining knowledge as perfecting an act fails.

Before we can define knowledge, we must consider objections. The commentator raises an important challenge: if knowledge is defined as perfecting an act, then knowledge of God and impossibles would be excluded because they are not acts. How does the defender respond? Let's examine the objection and its response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the Arabic passage where the objector raises the issue. Note that he concedes something about the servant's act but then points out what knowledge includes.
The objector concedes the act of the servant but argues that knowledge covers more than acts.
2
Understand the objector's reasoning
The objector says that whatever the servant brings into existence (act) is the object of knowledge only if it is an act. But knowledge includes things like knowledge of God and impossibles, which are not acts. Therefore, the definition (knowledge as perfecting an act) fails.
3
See the response
The defender responds by distinguishing between two senses of 'perfecting': perfecting the specific object versus perfecting in general. If the intended meaning is general perfection, the objection does not apply.
This distinction saves the definition from the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أورد عليه بعد تسليم أن فعل العبد بإيجاده ( علم أحدنا بنفسه وبالباري) تعالى وبالمستحيل فإن ما تعلق به هذا العلم ليس فعلا ولا مما يصح إتفانه به ( وإنما يرد ) عليه هذا ( إن لو أراد ما يصح به إتقان متعلقه وأما لو أراد ما يصح به ) الإتقان (في الجملة ) وإن لم يكن مصححا بحسب شخصه ( فلا) ورود لهذا عليه ( ولهم عبارات قريبة من (هذا العبارات المذكورة ) نحو تبيين المعلوم على ما هو به، وفيه الزيادة المذكورة والدور وأن التبيين مشعر بالظهور بعد الخفاء، فيخرج عنه علمه تعالى ( أو إثباته ) أي إثبات المعلوم على ما هو به وفيه الزيادة ،والدور وأنه يلزم أن يكون العالم بوجوده تعالى مثبتاً له، وهو محال وأيضاً الإثبات يطلق على الإيجاد وعلى تسكين الشيء عن الحركة ولا مجال ها هنا لإرادة شيء منهما ، وقد يطلق علم العلم تجوزاً، فيلزم تعريف الشيء بنفسه ( أو الثقة بأنه ( أي المعلوم ) على ما هو به ) وفيه الزيادة والدور، وإنه يوجب كون الباري تعالى واثقاً بما هو عالم به، وذلك مما يمتنع إطلاقه عليه شرعاً
Objection was raised after conceding that the act of the servant by his bringing into existence (knowledge of oneself and of the Creator) exalted and of the impossible, for what this knowledge is attached to is not an act nor something whose perfection is possible. (And it is only objected) to him this (if he intended what makes perfect its object, but if he intended what makes perfect) perfection (in general) even if not perfecting with respect to its specific thing, then no objection arises. And they have expressions close to these mentioned expressions, such as: making clear the known as it is, and in it is the mentioned addition and circularity, and that 'making clear' implies appearing after being hidden, so it excludes His knowledge, exalted. Or: establishing it, i.e., establishing the known as it is, and in it is the addition and circularity, and that it necessitates that the one who knows His existence, exalted, must be an establisher of Him, which is impossible. Also, 'establishing' is used for bringing into existence and for settling something from motion, and there is no room here to intend either of them. And sometimes 'knowledge of knowledge' is used metaphorically, so it necessitates defining a thing by itself. Or: trust that it (i.e., the known) is as it is, and in it is the addition and circularity, and that it necessitates that the Creator, exalted, be trusting in what He knows, and that is something impossible to attribute to Him by law.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to a specific definition of knowledge and then examines three alternative definitions. The author begins with an objection to a definition that apparently defined knowledge as 'the perfecting (itqān) of an act' or something similar. The objector says that knowledge includes things that are not acts (e.g., knowledge of God, of impossibles), so the definition fails. The response distinguishes between perfecting the specific object vs. perfecting in general. Then three quasi-synonymous definitions are listed: (1) making clear (tabīn) the known as it is, (2) establishing (ithbāt) the known as it is, and (3) trusting (thiqa) that it is as it is. Each is shown to have problems: they imply circularity (dawr), inappropriate implications for God, or linguistic misuse. For example, 'tabīn' implies prior hiddenness, which does not apply to God's knowledge; 'ithbāt' would mean the knower makes God's existence established, which is impossible; 'thiqa' implies trust, which is unsuitable for God. These criticisms demonstrate the difficulty of defining 'ilm in a way that is both comprehensive and theologically sound.

Key Terms
علم
إتقان
فعل
مستحيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the objector claim about knowledge of God and impossibles?
A) They are acts of the servant.
B) They are not acts and therefore not covered by the itqan definition.
C) They are perfections.
D) They are impossible to know.

Key Takeaway
The objection shows that defining knowledge as itqan requires careful specification of what 'perfecting' means.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You define knowledge as the ability to perfect acts. But what about your knowledge of God—does it perfect your worship? When you learn about His attributes, does it translate into deeper devotion, or is it just information? List one specific area where your knowledge of God has not changed your practice, and commit to turning it into action today.
?
The objection shows that not all knowledge is about doing. Consider what you know that has no immediate application—like knowledge of the impossible (e.g., that God cannot have a partner). Does this knowledge still carry weight? Are you accountable for it even if it never leads to a deed? How does this change your view of learning for learning's sake?
?
If knowledge is meant to perfect, then every concept you hold should refine your character. Name one belief you have about God or the unseen that has not yet shaped your daily choices. What is stopping you from letting that knowledge perfect your heart? Write a concrete step you will take within 24 hours to bridge that gap.

Three Alternative Definitions Criticized
Three definitions of knowledge (tabiyin, ithbat, thiqa) with their criticisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why three alternative definitions of knowledge – tabiyin, ithbat, and thiqa – are rejected due to circularity and theological incompatibility.

Core Idea
Defining knowledge as making clear, establishing, or trusting each leads to circularity or improper implications for God.

After dealing with one definition, the commentator lists three more that might seem plausible. Each one, however, suffers from similar problems: they either add an extra element, fall into circular reasoning, or imply something inappropriate about God. Let's examine each in turn.

Guided Path
1
Read the three definitions
Read the Arabic passage that begins with 'ولهم عبارات قريبة...' and identify the three definitions: tabyin, ithbat, thiqa.
Each is presented with its criticism.
2
For each, identify the criticism
For tabyin: it implies prior hiddenness, excluding God. For ithbat: it implies the knower creates the known, which is impossible for God. For thiqa: it implies trust, which is inappropriate for God.
Common themes: addition (زيادة), circularity (دور), and theological inappropriateness.
3
Understand why circularity is fatal
Each definition uses a term that itself requires knowledge to understand, leading to a circular definition. For example, 'making clear' presupposes the known is already clear.
Circularity makes the definition useless.
4
See the theological issue
Each definition, if applied to God, attributes something unsuitable (e.g., God's knowledge would imply hiddenness, creation, or trust).
This shows the definitions are not universally applicable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

How can you replace that unstable 'trust' with a certainty that God's knowledge is perfect and independent of your emotional state?
?

Al-Razi’s Definition of Knowledge: Conditions of Firmness and Cause
Al-Razi defines knowledge as firm belief based on a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's definition of knowledge and how its conditions of firm belief (jāzim) and causation (mūjib) refine the concept.

Core Idea
Knowledge is firm, correct belief that comes from a valid source.

Al-Razi's definition is one of the most precise in kalām. It adds the condition that knowledge must arise from a cause, distinguishing it from mere correct belief. This lesson unpacks each condition and shows how they protect the definition from common errors.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the parentheses and technical terms.
2
Identify the three conditions in the definition
The definition includes three implicit conditions: firmness (jāzim), correspondence (muṭābiq), and causation (mūjib). Find where each is mentioned.
The passage focuses on jāzim and mūjib.
3
Understand why 'jāzim' excludes compound ignorance and blind following
Firmness eliminates doubt and ensures the belief is not based on ignorance or imitation of the mistaken.
The text explicitly states this.
4
Understand why 'mūjib' excludes correct imitation
Even if a belief is correct, if it arises from imitation (taqlīd) of a correct source, the correspondence is coincidental, not caused by the proof.
This distinguishes knowledge from lucky guesses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : للإمام الرازي ( أنه ( اعتقاد جازم مطابق لموجب ) إما ضرورةً، أو دليل وإنما عرفه به بعد تنزله عن كونه ضرورياً) ولا غبار عليه غير أنه يخرج عنه التصور لعدم اندراجه في الاعتقاد، ولا يخفي وروده أيضاً على التعريف الأول المنقول عن بعض المعتزلة ( مع أنه علم يقال : ( مثلا في الأعراض ( علمت معنى المثلث و) في الجواهر علمت ) حقيقة الإنسان ) أو أراد أن الأول من المفهومات الاصطلاحية، دليل فقيد الجزم لإخراج الجهل المركب، وتقليد المخطئ ولموجب لإخراج تقليد المصيب، فإن الاعتقاد وإن كان ناشئاً عن الدليل عن قول المقلد لكن مطابقته ليس ناشئاً منه بل اتفاقي وقد قوله : ( وذلك مما يمتنع إطلاقه عليه تعالى شرعاً أجيب عنه بأن امتناع إطلاقه عليه تعالى شرعاً لكون أسمائه توقيفية وذلك لا يستلزم امتناع إطلاقه عليه لغةً، وهو المراد ها هنا وقد يقال : الوثوق مشعر بأنه فيما يحتمل غيره فثبت الامتناع مطلقاً. قوله : ( لموجب) فإن قلت : إن أراد الموجب الصحيح فلا حاجة إلى قيد المطابقة، وإن أراد الأعم يدخل الاعتقاد الجازم المطابق لموجب فاسد كأدلة أهل الحق الضعيفة مع ثبات قطعاً لجواز زواله عند العلم بفساد الدليل، وقد قالوا: إن الثبات هو المعتبر في العلم قلت المراد هو الأول ، وقيد المطابقة لأنها المعتبره في ماهية العلم لا للاحتراز . قوله غير أنه يخرج عنه التصور فإن قلت لعله خصص العلم بالتصديقات كما هو المشهور قلت التخصيص بها أمر حادث اصطلاحي والمقصود تعريف ماهية العلم، ولا كذلك تخصيصه بما سوى إدراك الجزئيات كما سيذكره هذا، واعترض على قوله ولا غبار إلخ بأنه يخرج علم الله تعالى أيضاً إذ لا يسمى اعتقاداً فلا يصح قوله لا غبار عليه غير خروج التصور، وأجيب بأن التعريف للعلم الحادث المنقسم إلى الضروري والكسبي والتصور والتصديق فلا ضير في خروج علمه تعالى، وفيه أنه اعترض على تعريف القاضي بخروجه فيندفع بهذا اعتراضه عنه أيضاً ، إلا أن يثبت وجود قرينة التخصيص في تعريف الإمام دون القاضي، ودونه خرط القتاد ويمكن أن يدعى ميل الإمام إلى مذهب المعتزلة في كون علمه تعالى عين ذاته كما أشار إليه المحقق التفتازاني في إلهيات المقاصد، فحينئذ لا غبار فتأمل وأما حديث تخصيص العلم المعرف بالحادث بعد القول بالعلم القديم ففيه أنه لا يناسب المقام لأن تصور العلم من المبادي التصورية فإن مسألة إثبات العلم للواجب مستدعية ضرورة لزوم تصور المحمول في التصديق، فالمناسب أن يجعل العلم المعرف المصدر بمباحث فن الكلام شاملاً للإلهي ، اللهم إلا أن يقال : ليس تعريفهم للعلم بما ذكر في أوائل الكتب الكلامية فتأمل. قوله : ( لعدم اندراجه في الاعتقاد إذ لا يقال : اعتقدت معنى المثلث وما يقال : من أن معنى اعتقاد الشيء اقتناؤه، واتخاذه في القلب لا ما يرادف التصديق على ما عليه الاصطلاح، ولهذا لم يحكم المصنف في التعريف الأول بخروج التصور مطلقاً، وإنما حكم به في هذا التعريف لأن الجازم بل المطابق أيضاً لا يكون إلا في النسبة لا لأن الاعتقاد، لا يشمله فتعسف محض يأباه مقام التعريف .
Fifth: from Imam al-Razi (that it is) a firm belief (i'tiqād jāzim) corresponding (muṭābiq) to a cause (mūjib), either by necessity or proof, and he defined it thus after descending from it being necessary. There is no flaw in it except that it excludes conceptual knowledge (taṣawwur) because it is not included in belief, and its applicability is also not hidden on the first definition transmitted from some Mu'tazila (with that it is knowledge said: for example in accidents: I knew the meaning of triangle, and in substances: I knew the reality of man) or he intended that the first is from the conventional concepts. The condition of firmness (jāzim) excludes compound ignorance and imitation of the mistaken, and the condition of cause (mūjib) excludes imitation of the correct, for the belief, even if it arises from the proof from the saying of the imitator, its correspondence does not arise from it but is coincidental. And his saying: (and that is impossible to attribute to Him exalted by law) was answered that the impossibility of attributing it to Him exalted by law is because His names are tawqīfī, and that does not necessitate impossibility of attributing it to Him linguistically, and that is the intended meaning here. And it may be said: trust (wuthūq) implies that it is in what admits of other, so impossibility is established absolutely. His saying: (to a cause) for if you say: if he intended the correct cause, then there is no need for the condition of correspondence, and if he intended the general, then it includes firm belief corresponding to a corrupt cause like the weak proofs of the people of truth with certainty of its steadiness because of the possibility of its removal upon knowledge of the corruption of the proof, and they have said that steadiness is what is considered in knowledge. I say: the intended is the first, and the condition of correspondence is because it is considered in the essence of knowledge, not for exclusion. His saying: except that it excludes conceptual knowledge, for if you say: perhaps he specified knowledge to assents (taṣdīqāt) as is well-known, I say: specification to them is a recent conventional matter, and the intent is definition of the essence of knowledge, and similarly its specification to other than perception of particulars as he will mention. And objection was made to his saying 'there is no flaw' etc. that it excludes God's knowledge as well, for it is not called belief, so his saying 'there is no flaw except exclusion of conceptual knowledge' is not sound. And it was answered that the definition is for contingent knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ḥādith) divided into necessary and acquired, concept and assent, so there is no harm in excluding His knowledge, exalted. And in it is that he objected to the Qāḍī's definition by its exclusion, so this answer also refutes his objection against it, unless there is evidence of specification in Imam's definition not in Qāḍī's, and that is extremely difficult. And it can be claimed that the Imam inclines to the Mu'tazilite school in that His knowledge is His essence, as alluded to by al-Taftāzānī in Ilāhiyyāt al-Maqāṣid, so then there is no flaw. And as for the talk of specifying the defined knowledge to the contingent after the statement of eternal knowledge, it is inappropriate to the context because the concept of knowledge is among the conceptual preliminaries, for the question of establishing knowledge for the Necessary requires necessarily the concept of the predicate in the assent. So it is appropriate to make the defined knowledge as the source in the investigations of the art of kalām including the divine, unless it is said: their definition of knowledge is not what is mentioned in the beginnings of the kalām books. His saying: (because it is not included in belief, for it is not said: I believed the meaning of triangle) and what is said: that the meaning of belief of a thing is its acquisition and taking in the heart, not synonymous with assent according to convention, and therefore the author did not judge in the first definition the exclusion of conceptual knowledge absolutely, but he judged it in this definition because the firm, rather also the corresponding, only occurs in the relation, not because belief does not include it, so it is pure arbitrariness which the context of definition rejects.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage presents Imam al-Razi's definition of knowledge as 'a firm belief (i'tiqād jāzim) that corresponds to reality (muṭābiq) and arises from a cause (mūjib), either necessarily or by proof.' The author explains each condition: 'jāzim' excludes compound ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab) and blind following of the mistaken; 'muṭābiq' is essential to the nature of knowledge though not for exclusion; 'mūjib' excludes correct imitation (taqlīd al-muṣīb) where the belief happens to be correct but not derived from a proper cause. Then the passage addresses the objection that this definition excludes conceptual knowledge (taṣawwur) since concepts are not 'beliefs'. The author notes that technical terminology limits 'ilm to assents (taṣdīqāt) but the definition is for absolute knowledge. Another objection is that it excludes God's knowledge since His knowledge is not called 'belief'. The response is that the definition is for contingent (ḥādith) knowledge, which is divided into necessary and acquired, concept and assent, so divine knowledge is rightfully excluded. The passage further discusses whether al-Razi intended to define only human knowledge, and whether his definition implies a Mu'tazilite view that God's knowledge is His essence. The author concludes with a critique of those who claim that the definition fails to include divine knowledge, suggesting that the context of kalam requires the definition to encompass divine knowledge, but this is a matter of debate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Razi's definition excludes conceptual knowledge (taṣawwur) because it is not a belief.
Correction: The definition is for absolute knowledge, but in technical usage, 'belief' is often limited to assents (taṣdīqāt). Conceptual knowledge is excluded by convention, not by the definition's failure.

Checkpoint
What does the condition 'جازم' in al-Razi's definition exclude?
A) Knowledge of God.
B) Compound ignorance and imitation of the mistaken.
C) Conceptual knowledge.
D) Necessary knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's definition uses three conditions to precisely define knowledge, but it excludes conceptual knowledge and divine knowledge by focusing on human acquired knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Razi insists that knowledge must be caused by a proper proof. Are your core beliefs about Allah and the unseen based on genuine evidence, or are they merely inherited from your environment? Will you examine them today with honesty?
?
He excludes correct imitation because its truth is coincidental. In your life, are you satisfied with being 'correct by accident' in matters of faith, or will you seek certainty from its true sources?
?
The definition excludes compound ignorance—being certain of falsehood. Is there an area where you hold a belief with confidence but have never questioned it? Would you be willing to let the evidence lead you, even if it unsettles you?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
10 min
Al-Qadi's definition of knowledge and its conditions
Al-Jurjani presents Al-Qadi's definition of knowledge and explains its conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Qadi's definition of knowledge as firm belief corresponding to a valid cause, and the rationale behind each of its three conditions.

Core Idea
Knowledge is certain belief that matches reality because of a sound cause.

In this passage, Al-Jurjani presents the definition of knowledge given by Al-Qadi, which is widely discussed in kalam. The definition has three key components: certainty (jazm), correspondence (mutabaqa), and causation (mujib). Each condition serves to exclude false claimants to knowledge such as compound ignorance and blind imitation. We will see how Al-Jurjani justifies each part of the definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage carefully and then the translation. Notice the structure: the author first states the definition, then explains each qualification.
2
Identify the three conditions
Find where the author discusses certainty (jazm), the cause (mujib), and correspondence (mutabaqa). Note how each is defined.
The passage also contains a short objection about the prohibition of applying this definition to Allah, which is answered.
3
Understand the rationale for certainty
The condition of certainty excludes compound ignorance (jahlu murakkab) and imitation of the mistaken (taqlid al-mukhti). Why is compound ignorance not knowledge?
Because it is a false belief held with certainty.
4
Understand the rationale for cause and correspondence
The condition of cause excludes imitation of the correct because the correspondence in imitation is accidental. Correspondence is essential to the definition, not just a precaution.
Notice the discussion about whether the cause must be sound or general.

دليل فقيد الجزم لإخراج الجهل المركب، وتقليد المخطئ ولموجب لإخراج تقليد المصيب، فإن الاعتقاد وإن كان ناشئاً عن الدليل عن قول المقلد لكن مطابقته ليس ناشئاً منه بل اتفاقي وقد

أنه ليس
أراد الأعم يدخل الاعتقاد الجازم المطابق لموجب فاسد كأدلة أهل الحق الضعيفة مع بثابت قطعاً لجواز زواله عند العلم بفساد الدليل، وقد قالوا: إن الثبات هو المعتبر في العلم قلت المراد هو الأول ، وقيد المطابقة لأنها المعتبره في ماهية العلم لا للاحتراز . قوله غير أنه يخرج عنه التصور فإن قلت لعله خصص العلم بالتصديقات كما هو المشهور قلت التخصيص بها أمر حادث اصطلاحي والمقصود تعريف ماهية العلم، ولا كذلك تخصيصه بما سوى إدراك الجزئيات كما سيذكره هذا، واعترض على قوله ولا غبار إلخ بأنه يخرج علم الله تعالى أيضاً إذ لا يسمى اعتقاداً فلا يصح قوله لا غبار عليه غير خروج التصور، وأجيب بأن التعريف للعلم الحادث المنقسم إلى الضروري والكسبي والتصور والتصديق فلا ضير في خروج علمه تعالى، وفيه أنه اعترض على تعريف القاضي بخروجه فيندفع بهذا اعتراضه عنه أيضاً ، إلا أن يثبت وجود قرينة التخصيص في تعريف الإمام دون القاضي، ودونه خرط القتاد ويمكن أن يدعى ميل الإمام إلى مذهب المعتزلة في كون علمه تعالى عين ذاته كما أشار إليه المحقق التفتازاني في إلهيات المقاصد، فحينئذ لا غبار فتأمل وأما حديث تخصيص العلم المعرف بالحادث بعد القول بالعلم القديم ففيه أنه لا يناسب المقام لأن تصور العلم من المبادي التصورية فإن مسألة إثبات العلم للواجب مستدعية ضرورة لزوم تصور المحمول في التصديق، فالمناسب أن يجعل العلم المعرف المصدر بمباحث فن الكلام شاملاً للإلهي ، اللهم إلا أن يقال : ليس تعريفهم للعلم بما ذكر في أوائل الكتب الكلامية فتأمل. قوله : ( لعدم اندراجه في الاعتقاد إذ لا يقال : اعتقدت معنى المثلث وما يقال : من أن معنى اعتقاد الشيء اقتناؤه، واتخاذه في القلب لا ما يرادف التصديق على ما عليه الاصطلاح، ولهذا لم يحكم المصنف في التعريف الأول بخروج التصور مطلقاً، وإنما حكم به في هذا التعريف لأن الجازم بل المطابق أيضاً لا يكون إلا في النسبة لا لأن الاعتقاد، لا يشمله فتعسف محض يأباه مقام
التعريف .
The Second Observatory – On the Definition of Absolute Knowledge. A proof: He only defined it after descending from its being necessary. And there is no dust on it except that it excludes conception because it is not included in belief. And its occurrence on the first definition transmitted from some Mu'tazila is not hidden, (along with the fact that it is said: for example, in accidents, 'I knew the meaning of triangle,' and in substances, 'I knew the reality of man'), or he intended that the first is of conventional concepts. A proof: the condition of certainty is to exclude compound ignorance and the imitation of the mistaken one, and the condition of cause is to exclude the imitation of the correct one. For belief, even if it arises from the statement of an imitator, its correspondence does not arise from it but is accidental. His statement: (And that [the definition] is such that its application to Him, exalted, is prohibited by sacred law) was answered: that the prohibition of its application to Him by sacred law is because His names are tawqifiyya (derived from revelation), and that does not entail the prohibition of its application to Him according to language, and that is what is intended here. And it may be said: confidence suggests that it [the definition] concerns what admits of its opposite, so prohibition is established absolutely. His statement: (to a cause). For if you say: if He intends the sound cause, then there is no need for the condition of correspondence; and if He intends the general [cause], then there enters [under the definition] firm belief corresponding to an invalid cause, like the weak proofs of the people of truth, but it is not established as certain because it is possible for it to disappear upon knowledge of the soundness of the proof. And they said: firmness is what is considered in knowledge. I say: what is intended is the first [sound cause], and the condition of correspondence is because it is considered in the essence of knowledge, not as a safeguard. His statement: except that it excludes conception. For if you say: perhaps he restricted knowledge to assents (tasdiqat) as is well-known. I say: restriction to them is a recent conventional matter, and the intent is the definition of the essence of knowledge, and not likewise is restriction to other than perception of particulars as he will mention. This: and objection was raised against his statement 'no dust on it' etc. that it also excludes the knowledge of Allah, since it is not called belief, so his statement 'no dust on it except for the exclusion of conception' is not valid. And it was answered: that the definition is for originated knowledge which divides into necessary and acquired, and conception and assent, so there is no harm in excluding His knowledge. And it is said: he objected to al-Qadi's definition by its [the definition's] exclusion [of divine knowledge], so this objection is repelled by the same [answer from limitation to originated knowledge]. Unless the existence of a contextual indicator of restriction is established in the definition of al-Imam but not al-Qadi, and that is very difficult. It is possible to claim that al-Imam inclined to the Mu'tazilite doctrine that His knowledge is identical to His essence, as al-Muhaqqiq al-Taftazani indicated in the Ilahiyyat of al-Maqasid. Then there is no dust, so reflect. As for the matter of restricting the defined knowledge to originated after the statement of eternal knowledge, it is said about it that it does not suit the context, because the concept of knowledge is among conceptual premises, for the issue of proving knowledge for the Necessary Being necessitates the necessity of conceiving the predicate in the judgment. So it is appropriate that the knowledge defined in the discussions of the art of kalam be inclusive of the divine, unless it is said: their definition of knowledge is not what is mentioned in the beginnings of the books of kalam, so reflect. His statement: (because it is not included in belief, since it is not said: 'I believed the meaning of triangle'). And what is said: that the meaning of belief in something is its acquisition and taking in the heart, not what is synonymous with assent according to convention. For this reason, the author did not judge in the first definition [quoted] the exclusion of conception absolutely, but only judged it in this definition [of al-Qadi], because the certain – indeed the corresponding – is only in a relation, not because belief does not include it [conception]. That is pure arbitrariness that the context of definition rejects.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the definition of knowledge as 'firm belief corresponding to a valid cause' (al-i'tiqad al-jazim al-mutabiq li-mujib), attributed to Al-Qadi. The author begins by noting that this definition is proposed after conceding that knowledge is not a necessary concept. He states that the definition is sound except that it excludes conception (tasawwur), since conception is not included under 'belief'. He then notes that a similar objection applies to the first definition transmitted from some Mu'tazilites. The definition is then analyzed: the condition of certainty (jazm) excludes compound ignorance and the imitation of the mistaken, and the condition of a cause (mujib) excludes the imitation of the correct person, because although the belief may arise from a statement, its correspondence with reality is coincidental rather than caused by the proof. An objection is raised that applying this definition to Allah is prohibited by sacred law because divine names are tawqifiyya. The answer is that the prohibition is only by revelation, not by language, and the definition is intended linguistically. Another objection concerns the term 'mujib': if it means the correct cause, then the additional condition of correspondence (mutabaqa) is redundant; if it means any cause, then weak proofs would be included. The response clarifies that 'mujib' here means the correct cause, and the condition of correspondence is essential to the definition, not merely precautionary. The main objection about excluding conception is then addressed: one might think the definition implicitly restricts knowledge to assent (tasdiq) as is common among theologians. The author responds that such restriction is a later convention, while the aim here is to define the essence of knowledge. He also rejects the idea that the definition excludes perception of particulars, deferring that to a later discussion. A further objection states that the definition also excludes Allah's knowledge since His knowledge is not called 'belief'. The answer is that the definition is for originated knowledge (al-ilm al-hadith), which is divided into necessary and acquired, conception and assent; divine knowledge is eternal and outside this scope. The passage then compares Al-Qadi's definition to that of Al-Imam (Al-Ash'ari?), suggesting that the objection is repelled if one accepts that the context restricts the definition to originated knowledge. However, it is noted that Al-Imam may have inclined to the Mu'tazilite view that Allah's knowledge is identical to His essence, in which case there is no issue. The passage concludes by critiquing the attempt to restrict the definition to originated knowledge on the grounds that the concept of knowledge is a conceptual premise needed for proving divine knowledge; thus the definition should encompass the divine. Nonetheless, the author notes that perhaps the definition given in early kalam books was not intended to be universal. Finally, the author explains why conception is excluded: one does not say 'I believed the meaning of triangle'; belief means acquisition in the heart, not synonymous with assent. The author criticizes the reasoning that the certain is only in a relation, not that belief excludes conception, as arbitrary.

Key Takeaway
Al-Qadi defines knowledge as firm belief that corresponds to reality due to a valid cause, with each condition eliminating a specific error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you have certain belief (jazm) in the existence of Allah that is based on a sound cause (mujib sahih), or are you merely imitating your parents or society? What would it take for you to verify that foundation?
?
If your knowledge of Allah's attributes is merely inherited or based on weak proofs, how secure is it? Could it be shaken by doubt? What steps will you take to ensure your knowledge is firm and correctly corresponds to reality?
?
The condition of mutabaqa means your belief must match reality. Have you ever checked if your understanding of Allah aligns with what He has revealed? Or have you built a concept of Allah that is convenient but perhaps not accurate?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate

Defending Al-Qadi's definition: objections and responses
Al-Jurjani responds to objections against Al-Qadi's definition regarding conception and divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Jurjani defends Al-Qadi's definition against objections concerning the exclusion of conception and divine knowledge.

Core Idea
Al-Qadi's definition is for created knowledge and uses 'belief' in a specific sense.

This passage addresses two major criticisms of Al-Qadi's definition. First, it seems to exclude conceptual knowledge (tasawwur) because conception is not called 'belief'. Second, it seems to exclude Allah's knowledge since His knowledge is not belief. Al-Jurjani responds by clarifying that the definition is limited to originated human knowledge and that 'belief' here means assent (tasdiq) by convention.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the entire passage to grasp the flow of objections and responses. Note the back-and-forth structure.
2
Identify the first objection
The first objection is that the definition excludes conception (tasawwur). Find how it is raised and the initial response.
The author says that restriction to assent is a later convention.
3
Identify the second objection
The second objection is that the definition cannot apply to Allah's knowledge. See how the response limits the definition to originated knowledge.
This leads to a discussion about the scope of definitions.
4
Follow the final critique
The passage ends with a critique of restricting the definition to originated knowledge and a reflection on the purpose of definitions in kalam.
This shows the depth of the debate.

التعريف .
The Second Observatory – On the Definition of Absolute Knowledge. A proof: He only defined it after descending from its being necessary. And there is no dust on it except that it excludes conception because it is not included in belief. And its occurrence on the first definition transmitted from some Mu'tazila is not hidden, (along with the fact that it is said: for example, in accidents, 'I knew the meaning of triangle,' and in substances, 'I knew the reality of man'), or he intended that the first is of conventional concepts. A proof: the condition of certainty is to exclude compound ignorance and the imitation of the mistaken one, and the condition of cause is to exclude the imitation of the correct one. For belief, even if it arises from the statement of an imitator, its correspondence does not arise from it but is accidental. His statement: (And that [the definition] is such that its application to Him, exalted, is prohibited by sacred law) was answered: that the prohibition of its application to Him by sacred law is because His names are tawqifiyya (derived from revelation), and that does not entail the prohibition of its application to Him according to language, and that is what is intended here. And it may be said: confidence suggests that it [the definition] concerns what admits of its opposite, so prohibition is established absolutely. His statement: (to a cause). For if you say: if He intends the sound cause, then there is no need for the condition of correspondence; and if He intends the general [cause], then there enters [under the definition] firm belief corresponding to an invalid cause, like the weak proofs of the people of truth, but it is not established as certain because it is possible for it to disappear upon knowledge of the soundness of the proof. And they said: firmness is what is considered in knowledge. I say: what is intended is the first [sound cause], and the condition of correspondence is because it is considered in the essence of knowledge, not as a safeguard. His statement: except that it excludes conception. For if you say: perhaps he restricted knowledge to assents (tasdiqat) as is well-known. I say: restriction to them is a recent conventional matter, and the intent is the definition of the essence of knowledge, and not likewise is restriction to other than perception of particulars as he will mention. This: and objection was raised against his statement 'no dust on it' etc. that it also excludes the knowledge of Allah, since it is not called belief, so his statement 'no dust on it except for the exclusion of conception' is not valid. And it was answered: that the definition is for originated knowledge which divides into necessary and acquired, and conception and assent, so there is no harm in excluding His knowledge. And it is said: he objected to al-Qadi's definition by its [the definition's] exclusion [of divine knowledge], so this objection is repelled by the same [answer from limitation to originated knowledge]. Unless the existence of a contextual indicator of restriction is established in the definition of al-Imam but not al-Qadi, and that is very difficult. It is possible to claim that al-Imam inclined to the Mu'tazilite doctrine that His knowledge is identical to His essence, as al-Muhaqqiq al-Taftazani indicated in the Ilahiyyat of al-Maqasid. Then there is no dust, so reflect. As for the matter of restricting the defined knowledge to originated after the statement of eternal knowledge, it is said about it that it does not suit the context, because the concept of knowledge is among conceptual premises, for the issue of proving knowledge for the Necessary Being necessitates the necessity of conceiving the predicate in the judgment. So it is appropriate that the knowledge defined in the discussions of the art of kalam be inclusive of the divine, unless it is said: their definition of knowledge is not what is mentioned in the beginnings of the books of kalam, so reflect. His statement: (because it is not included in belief, since it is not said: 'I believed the meaning of triangle'). And what is said: that the meaning of belief in something is its acquisition and taking in the heart, not what is synonymous with assent according to convention. For this reason, the author did not judge in the first definition [quoted] the exclusion of conception absolutely, but only judged it in this definition [of al-Qadi], because the certain – indeed the corresponding – is only in a relation, not because belief does not include it [conception]. That is pure arbitrariness that the context of definition rejects.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the definition of knowledge as 'firm belief corresponding to a valid cause' (al-i'tiqad al-jazim al-mutabiq li-mujib), attributed to Al-Qadi. The author begins by noting that this definition is proposed after conceding that knowledge is not a necessary concept. He states that the definition is sound except that it excludes conception (tasawwur), since conception is not included under 'belief'. He then notes that a similar objection applies to the first definition transmitted from some Mu'tazilites. The definition is then analyzed: the condition of certainty (jazm) excludes compound ignorance and the imitation of the mistaken, and the condition of a cause (mujib) excludes the imitation of the correct person, because although the belief may arise from a statement, its correspondence with reality is coincidental rather than caused by the proof. An objection is raised that applying this definition to Allah is prohibited by sacred law because divine names are tawqifiyya. The answer is that the prohibition is only by revelation, not by language, and the definition is intended linguistically. Another objection concerns the term 'mujib': if it means the correct cause, then the additional condition of correspondence (mutabaqa) is redundant; if it means any cause, then weak proofs would be included. The response clarifies that 'mujib' here means the correct cause, and the condition of correspondence is essential to the definition, not merely precautionary. The main objection about excluding conception is then addressed: one might think the definition implicitly restricts knowledge to assent (tasdiq) as is common among theologians. The author responds that such restriction is a later convention, while the aim here is to define the essence of knowledge. He also rejects the idea that the definition excludes perception of particulars, deferring that to a later discussion. A further objection states that the definition also excludes Allah's knowledge since His knowledge is not called 'belief'. The answer is that the definition is for originated knowledge (al-ilm al-hadith), which is divided into necessary and acquired, conception and assent; divine knowledge is eternal and outside this scope. The passage then compares Al-Qadi's definition to that of Al-Imam (Al-Ash'ari?), suggesting that the objection is repelled if one accepts that the context restricts the definition to originated knowledge. However, it is noted that Al-Imam may have inclined to the Mu'tazilite view that Allah's knowledge is identical to His essence, in which case there is no issue. The passage concludes by critiquing the attempt to restrict the definition to originated knowledge on the grounds that the concept of knowledge is a conceptual premise needed for proving divine knowledge; thus the definition should encompass the divine. Nonetheless, the author notes that perhaps the definition given in early kalam books was not intended to be universal. Finally, the author explains why conception is excluded: one does not say 'I believed the meaning of triangle'; belief means acquisition in the heart, not synonymous with assent. The author criticizes the reasoning that the certain is only in a relation, not that belief excludes conception, as arbitrary.

Correction: The definition is specifically for originated (created) human knowledge, so it does not need to encompass divine knowledge.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The scholars debated definitions rigorously to protect the boundaries of knowledge. In your own life, are you as careful about what you claim to know about Allah? Are there beliefs you hold based solely on hearsay rather than deeper reflection? Commit today to verifying one belief through study.

The philosophers' definition and its inclusive objection
Al-Jurjani presents the philosophers' definition of knowledge and argues it violates linguistic custom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' definition of knowledge and why it is criticized for including states that are not considered knowledge in ordinary language.

Core Idea
The philosophers' definition of knowledge is too broad: it includes even doubt and ignorance.

Al-Jurjani presents the sixth definition of knowledge, that of the philosophers, which equates knowledge with mental representation. This definition may seem comprehensive, but it leads to a serious problem: it includes mental states like conjecture and compound ignorance, which no one would call knowledge. He then argues that this violates linguistic custom, common usage, and religious law.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the two formulations: 'acquisition of the form of the thing in the intellect' and 'representation of the quiddity of the perceived in the soul of the perceiver'.
Note that the definition is meant to cover both universals and particulars.
2
Identify the inclusive objection
Find where Al-Jurjani lists the states that this definition erroneously includes: conjecture, compound ignorance, imitation, doubt, and illusion.
These are all mental states that are not knowledge.
3
Understand why this is problematic
He argues that calling these states 'knowledge' contradicts the usage of language, custom, and sacred law. For example, we never call a person with compound ignorance a knower.
Consider why this objection matters for defining knowledge.

والثاني من الماهيات الموجودة ( السادس للحكماء ) أنه (حصول صورة الشيء) كلياً كان أو جزئياً موجوداً أو معدوماً في العقل أي عنده ليتناول إدراك الجزئيات ) ويقال : ( بعبارة ظاهرة الاختصاص بالكليات ) هو تمثل ماهية المدرك ) بفتح الراء (في نفس المدرك بكسرها (وهو) أي كون العلم حصول الصورة، أو تمثل الماهية ( مبني على الوجود الذهني وسنبحث عنه ) أي عن الوجود الذهني وكون العلم عندهم عبارة عنه (وهذا) أي ما ذكروه في تعريف العلم ( يتناول الظن والجهل ) المركب ) والتقليد بل الشك والوهم) أيضاً ( وتسميتها علماً) أي جعلها مندرجة فيه كما ذهبوا إليه ( يخالف استعمال اللغة والعرف والشرع ) إذ لا يطلق على الجاهل جهلاً مركباً أنه عالم في شيء من استعمالات اللغة والعرف العام والشرع، كيف ويلزم
And the second of the existing essences (the sixth definition for the philosophers) is that (knowledge is) the acquisition of the form of the thing, whether universal or particular, existing or non-existent, in the intellect, i.e., with it (the intellect) so as to include the perception of particulars. And it is said: with an expression apparently specific to universals: it is the representation of the quiddity of the perceived (with fatha on the ra') in the soul of the perceiver (with kasra on the ra). And it (i.e., that knowledge is the acquisition of the form or the representation of the quiddity) is based on mental existence, and we will discuss it (i.e., mental existence and that knowledge according to them is an expression of it). And this (i.e., what they mentioned in defining knowledge) includes conjecture and (compound) ignorance and imitation, rather even doubt and illusion. And calling them knowledge (i.e., deeming them included in it as they held) contradicts the usage of language, custom, and sacred law. For one does not say of the one who is ignorantly compound ignorant that he is a knower in any of the usages of language, general custom, and sacred law. How so? And it necessitates...
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage introduces the philosophers' definition of knowledge, which is the sixth definition mentioned in the text. It is defined as 'the acquisition of the form of the thing in the intellect' (husul surat al-shay' fi al-'aql), whether the thing is universal or particular, existent or non-existent. The phrase 'in the intellect' (fi al-'aql) is interpreted to mean 'with it' ('indahu) to include perception of particulars. An alternative expression, phrased to appear specific to universals, is: 'the representation of the quiddity of the perceived in the soul of the perceiver' (tamaththul mahiyyat al-mudrak fi nafs al-mudrik). The passage notes that this definition is based on the concept of mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni), which will be discussed later. The main objection is that this definition is too broad: it includes conjecture (zann), compound ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab), imitation (taqlid), and even doubt (shakk) and illusion (wahm). Calling these 'knowledge' contradicts the common usage of language, custom ('urf), and sacred law (shar'), since one never describes a person with compound ignorance as 'knowledgeable'. The incomplete sentence 'how so? and it necessitates...' hints at further consequences that follow.

The Scope of 'Form' in the Philosophers' Definition
Al-Jurjani analyzes the term 'form' in the definition and its scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term 'form' (sura) in the philosophers' definition of knowledge can be interpreted to include or exclude presential knowledge, and its implications for divine knowledge.

Core Idea
The definition of knowledge hinges on whether 'form' includes external distinction or only mental representation.

Al-Jurjani begins his commentary by clarifying the pivotal term 'form' (sura) in the philosophers' definition. The meaning of 'form' determines whether the definition covers presential knowledge (ilm huduri) or only acquired knowledge (ilm husuli). This lesson unpacks the two interpretations and their theological consequences for understanding God's knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the first paragraph carefully, noticing the conditional structure: 'If by form is meant...' and 'If by it is meant...'.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Note the two meanings of sura: (1) that by which a thing is distinguished externally or mentally (includes presential), (2) that by which it is distinguished only in the mind (excludes presential).
3
Apply to divine knowledge
Consider how the first interpretation can accommodate divine knowledge (via forms in essence or intellects), but the second interpretation fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : حصول) صورة الشيء) إن أريد بالصورة ما به تميز الشيء في الخارج أو الذهن ليشمل العلم الحضوري أيضاً لأنه صورة خارجية، فكونه تعريفاً لمطلق العلم ظاهر، وكذا علم الواجب على القول يكون بحصول الصورة في ذاته تعالى كما في الإشارات، أو بحصولها في المجردات كما في شرحه، وأما على القول بكونه عين ذاته أو عبارة عن التجرد فلا، وإن أريد بها ما يميز به في الذهن على ما قيل : الأشياء في الخارج أعيان، وفي الذهن صور فهو مبني على نفي العلم الحضوري، وإن العلم بأنفسنا وصفاتنا النفسانية أيضاً حصولي .
قوله : ( أي عنده بناء على اعتبار التوسع في الظرفية بادعاء أن الحصول في آلات الشيء حصول فيه لكونه في تصرفه كما يقال : هذا المال في يد زيد لا أن في بمعنى مع على ما وهم، لأنه لا بد من حمله على مقارنة الحال للمحل فالإشكال بحاله .
قوله : (ظاهرة (الاختصاص أي بالنسبة إلى التعريف السابق وإن كلمة في وإن كانت ظاهرة في الظرفية الحقيقية لكنه يحتمل الظرفية التوسعية أيضاً، بخلاف في نفس المدرك بزيادة لفظ نفس فإنه لا يحتملها. قوله: (تمثل ماهية المدرك في نفس المدرك لم يعترض عليه بكونه دورياً بناء على ما ذكره المحقق في شرح الإشارات من أنه تعريف لفظي لا يتحاشى فيه عن لزوم الدور، إذ ليس الغرض تحصيل المجهول بل تعيين المعلوم.
قوله : (أي عنده لعل توجيهه على القاعدة أن يجعل في بمعنى مع كقوله تعالى : ادخلوا في أمم ﴾ [الأعراف: ۳۸] أي مع أمم، فيكون محصل معناه معنی عند ، وإلا فكون في بمعنى عند لم يذكر في كتب العربية.
قوله : (ظاهرة الاختصاص بالكليات فإن قلت : العبارة الأولى أيضاً ظاهرة الاختصاص بها فما الوجه في تخصيص ظهور الاختصاص بالثانية قلت : بعد تسليم ظهور الاختصاص في الأولى أيضاً، لا شك أن الظهور والخفاء أمران نسبيان فمراده أن العبارة الثانية ظاهرة الاختصاص
His statement: (acquisition) of the form of the thing). If by 'form' is meant that by which the thing is distinguished in external reality or in the mind, so as to include presential knowledge (al-'ilm al-huduri) also, because it is an external form, then its being a definition of absolute knowledge is apparent. Likewise the knowledge of the Necessary Being, according to the doctrine that it is by the acquisition of the form in His essence (may He be exalted) as in al-Isharat, or by its acquisition in the intellects as in its commentary; but according to the doctrine that it is identical to His essence or an expression of sheer intellectuality, then not. If by it is meant that by which it is distinguished in the mind, as it is said: things in external reality are entities (a'yan), and in the mind they are forms, then it is based on the denial of presential knowledge, and that knowledge of our selves and our psychic attributes is also acquired (husuli).
His statement: (i.e., with it) is based on considering metaphorical extension in the concept of containment, by claiming that acquisition in the instruments of a thing is acquisition in it because it is under its disposal, as it is said: 'this wealth is in the hand of Zayd', not that 'in' means 'with' as some have mistakenly thought, because it must be interpreted as the relation of state to location, so the problem remains as is.
His statement: (apparently specific to universals) i.e., relative to the previous definition; and although the word 'fi' (in) is apparently literal containment, it can also bear the metaphorical extension, unlike 'fi nafs al-mudrik' with the addition of the word 'nafs', which does not permit it. His statement: (the representation of the quiddity of the perceived in the soul of the perceiver) is not objected to as being circular, based on what the muhaqqiq mentioned in Sharh al-Isharat: that it is a verbal definition in which one does not avoid the occurrence of circularity, because the purpose is not to acquire the unknown but to specify the known.
His statement: (i.e., with it) perhaps its justification according to the rule is to make 'fi' mean 'ma'a' (with), as His saying (Exalted): 'Enter in among nations' (Quran 7:38), i.e., with nations. Then the resultant meaning is the meaning of 'inda (with/at). Otherwise, the fact that 'fi' means 'inda is not mentioned in the books of Arabic.
His statement: (apparently specific to universals). For if you say: the first expression is also apparently specific to them, so what is the point in specifying the appearance of specificity with the second? I say: after conceding the appearance of specificity in the first also, there is no doubt that appearance and hiddenness are relative matters; so his intent is that the second expression is apparently specific [to universals].
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed gloss on the wording of the philosophers' definition. The author starts by analyzing the phrase 'acquisition of the form of the thing' (husul surat al-shay'). If 'form' is understood as that which distinguishes the thing in external reality or in the mind, then the definition can include presential knowledge (al-ilm al-huduri), since presential knowledge is an external form. In that case, the definition applies to absolute knowledge, and even to the knowledge of the Necessary Being if one holds that He knows through forms in His essence (as in Ibn Sina's al-Isharat) or in the separate intellects. However, if one holds that divine knowledge is identical to His essence or pure intellectuality, then the definition does not apply. If 'form' is taken only as a mental representation, then the definition denies presential knowledge, and even our self-knowledge becomes acquired (husuli).
Next, the phrase 'fi al-'aql' (in the intellect) is interpreted as 'indahu' (with it), based on metaphorical extension (al-tawassu' fi al-dharrfiyya). The author explains that this means the acquisition is in the thing's instruments, so it is considered 'in' it, like saying 'the wealth is in Zayd's hand'. But this still requires a relation of state to location, so the problem remains.
Then, the alternative expression 'representation of the quiddity of the perceived in the soul of the perceiver' (tamaththul mahiyyat al-mudrak fi nafs al-mudrik) is said to be more explicitly specific to universals because the word 'nafs' prevents the metaphorical interpretation of 'fi'. The author then addresses a potential objection of circularity (dawr) in this definition: defining the perceived (mudrak) in terms of the perceiver (mudrik) might seem circular. The response, following al-Muhaqqiq in Sharh al-Isharat, is that this is a verbal definition (ta'rif lafzi) where circularity is permissible because the aim is not to discover something unknown but to specify the known concept.
The final part re-explains the interpretation of 'fi al-'aql' as 'inda' (with/at), suggesting that 'fi' might be taken to mean 'ma'a' (with), as in the Quranic verse 'adkhulu fi umamin' (enter with nations). If so, the meaning reduces to 'inda'. The author notes that 'fi' meaning 'inda' is not recorded in Arabic grammar books.
The last gloss discusses the apparent specificity of the second expression to universals. The author acknowledges that the first expression (fi al-'aql) is also apparently specific to universals, but argues that appearance is relative; the second expression is more clearly specific because 'nafs' removes ambiguity.

Key Terms
صورة
علم حضوري
علم حصولي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' definition of knowledge only applies to acquired knowledge (ilm husuli).
Correction: The definition can include presential knowledge if 'form' is interpreted broadly as that which distinguishes the thing in external reality or mind.

Checkpoint
According to the first interpretation of 'form', which type of knowledge is included?
A) Only acquired knowledge
B) Both acquired and presential knowledge
C) Only presential knowledge
D) Neither acquired nor presential

Key Takeaway
The term 'form' in the definition is ambiguous: it can either include presential knowledge (by referring to external distinction) or restrict to mental representation (excluding presential).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your knowledge of Allah, do you experience it as a direct presence (presential) or as a mental concept (acquired)? This lesson shows that the definition of knowledge you adopt affects this. Which interpretation better describes your actual experience, and why? (Be honest: do you feel His presence or only hold a belief?)
?
The passage suggests that even your knowledge of your own self could be considered acquired if form is only mental. Do you know yourself through direct awareness or through reflection and concepts? What does your answer reveal about whether you implicitly hold the first or second interpretation of 'form'?
