Resolving the Inclusion of Conception
Sanusi presents the correct interpretation and addresses objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct resolution: redefining 'attribute' as the intellectual form itself and applying the condition relative to the perceiver.

Core Idea
Conception and assent are themselves attributes that necessitate disclosure, with the condition applied to their objects relative to the perceiver.

After the initial attempt led to contradiction, the text presents the correct interpretation: 'attribute' means the intellectual form itself, and 'not admitting the contrary' is understood relative to the perceiver. This lesson unpacks that resolution and addresses further objections, including the distinction between essential and aspectual conception.

Guided Path
1
Read the resolution passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice how the author reinterprets 'attribute' and the condition to avoid the earlier contradiction.
The key move is to take 'attribute' as the intellectual form itself, not a separate entity.
2
Understand the relative condition
The condition 'does not admit the contrary' is applied to the object of distinction relative to the perceiver. For conception, the object has no contrary; for certain assent, the object has a contrary but the certitude excludes it.
This preserves the idea that both are knowledge without being mere distinctions.
3
Address the objection about conception having no contrary
The objection that even if conception had a contrary, its object would not admit it is answered by distinguishing between essential (bi’l-kunn) and aspectual (bi’l-wajh) conception.
Only aspectual conception can admit a contrary; essential cannot.
4
Note the commentator's adherence to the author's earlier view
The commentator did not adopt this resolution because he followed the author's earlier work, but the glosses offer a correction.
This shows the development of the doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المتفرع على الصفة إنما هو له لا للصفة ولا شك أن تمييزه إنما هو لشيء تتعلق به تلك الصفة والتمييز، وذلك الشيء هو الذي لا يحتمل النقيض، وهذا الحد يتناول التصديق اليقيني وهو ظاهر والتصور أيضاً، إذ لا نقيض له لأن المتناقضين هما المتصورة وفي التصديق النفي، أو الإثبات والمتعلق الطرفان كذا أفاده الشارح في حواشي شرح مختصر الأصول. وله : إذ لا نقيض (له أي لنميزه بناء على أن التصور والتصديق اليقيني عبارتان عما يوجب الصورة، والنفي والإثبات لكن ظاهر ،قوله ولا مانع بين التصورات فإن مفهومي الإنسان إلخ يأباه، فيحتاج إلى العناية في مواضع عديدة فالأظهر أن يؤول قوله وهذا الحد يتناول بمعنى يتناول ما يوجبهما، ويحتمل التصديق والتصور على المعنى المتعارف أعني الحكم والصورة. قوله : ( والتصور أيضاً إذ لا نقيض له أي لتمييزه على حذف المضاف إذ المعتبر في العلم عدم احتمال نقيض التمييز ثم التمييز في التصور نفس الصورة، والمتعلق الماهية المتصورة وفي التصديقات الإثبات أو النفي، والمتعلق الطرفان ولا يخفى أن الأولى لا نقيض لها، والأخيرين كل منهما نقيض الآخر كذا حققه الشارح في حواشي شرح العضد، فلا يرد لزوم أن لا يكون التصور علماً بل تمييزاً مترتباً على صفة هي العلم، وكذا الحال في التصديق لكن يلزم أن لا يكون التصديق نفس الإثبات والنفي، بل صفة موجبة لهما، وكذا أن لا يكون التصور نفس تلك الصورة، بل صفة موجبة لها وهذا مخالف لما تقرر عندهم على أنا لا نسلم أن لنا صفة موجبة توجب الإثبات والنفي والصورة العقلية بل ليس لنا في الواقع إلا أحدها، فالصواب أن يراد بالصفة نفس الصورة العقلية وبالتمييز المعنى المصدري، ويكون المعنى لا يحتمل متعلق ذلك التمييز نقيض تلك الصفة، إذ لا يحتمل متعلق التمييز نقيض نفسه بالقياس إلى المدرك، فمتعلق التمييز في التصور أعني المتصور لا نقيض له فلا يحتمله أصلاً، ومتعلق التصديق أعني وقوع النسبة في نفس الأمر له نقيض وهو لا وقوعها فيه، فكل واحد من التصور والتصديق صفة توجب انكشافاً وإيضاحاً، لا يحتمل متعلقه نقيضه بالقياس إلى المدرك أما التصور فظاهر، وأما التصديق لأنه إذا كان مطابقاً جازماً لم يحتمل بالقياس إليه، وإذا فات شيء من الصفات احتمله والشارح المحقق إنما لم يحمل التعريف على هذين الوجهين اتباعاً لما ذكره المصنف في شرح الأصول أن متعلق التمييز في التصديق الطرفان ، وإن المعتبر نقيض التمييز هذا، واعترض أيضاً على ما ذكره الشارح بأن كل متصور لا يحتمل غير صورته الخاصة، فلو سلم إن للتصور نقيضاً فمتعلقه لا يحتمل نقيضه فلا معنى للبناء على عدم النقيض، وأجيب بأن هذا في المتصور بالكنه لا في المتصور بالوجه، فإنه لو فرض أن اللاضاحك بالفعل نقيض الضاحك بالفعل، فلا شك أن الإنسان المتصور بأحدهما يحتمل أن يتصور بالآخر على أن بناء شيء على شيء في الواقع لا ينافي وجود مبنى آخر له في التقدير، وبما ذكرنا من أن المتميز في التصديق هو الإثبات والنفي كما صرح به الشارح في الحواشي يندفع اعتراض الأستاذ بأن المراد من النقيض النقيض المصطلح كما يدل من
What is derivative from the attribute belongs to it, not to the attribute, and there is no doubt that its distinction is only for something to which that attribute and the distinction are related, and that thing is what does not admit the contrary. This definition includes certain assent (al-taṣdīq al-yaqīnī) — that is apparent — and conception (al-taṣawwur) as well, since it has no contrary, because the two contradictories are the conceived things, while in assent it is negation or affirmation, and the object is the two extremes. Thus the commentator in the glosses of Sharh Mukhtasar al-Usul stated. And he has: since it has no contrary — i.e., for its distinction, based on the fact that conception and certain assent are expressions for what necessitates the form and the negation or affirmation. But apparently his saying 'and there is no obstacle between the conceptions', for the two concepts of man, etc., precludes it, so care is needed in many places. The more apparent is that his saying 'this definition includes' is interpreted in the sense of including what necessitates them, and it is possible that assent and conception are taken in the conventional sense, namely judgment and form. His saying: (and conception as well, since it has no contrary) — i.e., for its distinction, by deletion of the genitive, because what is considered in knowledge is the absence of the possibility of the contrary of the distinction. Then the distinction in conception is the form itself, and the object is the conceived quiddity. In assents, it is affirmation or negation, and the object is the two extremes. And it is not hidden that the former has no contrary, while the latter two are each the contrary of the other. Thus the commentator corroborated in the glosses of Sharh al-‘Adud. So the objection that conception would not be knowledge but rather a distinction consequent upon an attribute that is knowledge does not arise; similarly for assent. But it entails that assent is not itself affirmation or negation, but an attribute necessitating them, and that conception is not itself that form but an attribute necessitating it — and this contradicts what is established among them. Moreover, we do not accept that we have an necessitating attribute that necessitates affirmation, negation, and intellectual form; rather in reality we have only one of them. So the correct thing is that by 'attribute' is meant the intellectual form itself, and by 'distinction' the infinitive meaning. Thus the meaning is: the object of that distinction does not admit the contrary of that attribute, for the object of distinction does not admit its own contrary relative to the perceiver. The object of distinction in conception, namely the conceived object, has no contrary, so it does not admit it at all. The object of assent, namely the occurrence of the relation in actual fact, has a contrary, which is its non-occurrence in it. So each of conception and assent is an attribute that necessitates disclosure and clarification, such that its object does not admit its contrary relative to the perceiver. As for conception, it is apparent; as for assent, because if it is conformable and certain, it does not admit to its contrary relative to it, and if something of the attributes is missing, it admits it. The verifying commentator only did not apply the definition in these two ways in following what the author mentioned in Sharh al-Usul that the object of distinction in assent is the two extremes, and that what is considered is the contrary of the distinction. And there was also an objection to what the commentator mentioned: that every conceived object does not admit its own specific form, so if it is conceded that conception has a contrary, its object does not admit its contrary, so there is no meaning to building on the absence of a contrary. It was answered that this applies to the object conceived in essence (bi’l-kunn), not to the object conceived in aspect (bi’l-wajh). For if it is assumed that 'non-laugher in actuality' is the contrary of 'laugher in actuality', there is no doubt that the human conceived by one of them can be conceived by the other. And because building one thing upon another in reality does not preclude the existence of another basis for it in estimation. And by what we have mentioned, that what is distinguished in assent is affirmation and negation, as the commentator stated in the glosses, the objection of the master (al-ustadh) is refuted that what is meant by 'contrary' is the technical contrary, as indicated by...
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This extended passage addresses how the definition of knowledge includes both certain assent (al-taṣdīq al-yaqīnī) and conception (al-taṣawwur). The commentator explains that the definition applies to assent obviously because it involves a judgment that does not admit its opposite. For conception, it seems problematic because conception (like the form of a man) has no contrary. The text goes into detailed analysis, first presenting the view of the commentator in the glosses, then objections and alternative interpretations. The key difficulty is that if knowledge is defined as an attribute necessitating a distinction, then conception itself would only be a distinction consequent on an attribute, not the attribute itself. The author resolves this by reinterpreting 'attribute' as the intellectual form itself, not a separate entity. Then the condition 'does not admit the contrary' is understood relative to the perceiver: in conception, the perceived object has no contrary; in certain assent, the judgment (e.g., the occurrence of a relation) has a contrary, but the certitude excludes it. The passage also rebuts an objection that even if conception had a contrary, its object would not admit it, by distinguishing between essential and aspectual conception. Finally, the commentator follows the author's approach from Sharh al-Usul, but a later correction is offered based on the glosses.

Key Terms
الصورة العقلية
التمييز
المتصور بالكنه
المتصور بالوجه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the definition requires the object to have no contrary in absolute terms.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the condition is 'relative to the perceiver': for conception, the object indeed has no contrary; for assent, the object has a contrary but the certainty excludes it from the perceiver's standpoint.

Checkpoint
According to the correct interpretation, what does 'not admitting the contrary' mean for certain assent?
A) The object of assent has no contrary
B) The object of assent has a contrary, but the certainty excludes it relative to the perceiver
C) Assent itself has no contrary
D) The contrary is only considered in conception

Key Takeaway
Conception and certain assent are themselves attributes that necessitate disclosure, with their objects not admitting the contrary relative to the perceiver, resolving the contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate the Oneness of God (tawḥīd), is your conception of it essential (bi al-kunnh) or aspectual (bi al-wajh)? How does this distinction affect the certainty you have in that belief? Ask yourself: am I willing to let go of any aspectual understanding to reach an essential conception?
?
The passage states that certain assent does not admit its contrary relative to the perceiver. Consider your own certainty about the truth of the Shahada. Is there any point of doubt, even a flicker, that you have not confronted? What would it require to eliminate that possibility entirely from your perspective?

Definition of Absolute Knowledge and Its Coverage
Al-Iji defines absolute knowledge and includes both tasawwur and tasdiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of absolute knowledge as that which does not admit its opposite, and how this definition encompasses both tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent).

Core Idea
Knowledge is that whose object cannot have its opposite.

Al-Iji opens the second observation post by defining absolute knowledge. He argues that knowledge is a quality that brings about a distinction (tamyīz) for something that does not admit its opposite. He then claims this definition covers both certain assent (taṣdīq) and conception (taṣawwur). This passage establishes the foundational definition that all subsequent discussions of knowledge build upon.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Al-Iji defines knowledge not as a simple concept but as something derived from an attribute (ṣifa) that produces a distinction (tamyīz).
The definition is: 'that which does not admit the opposite'.
2
Understand what the definition covers
Al-Iji states that this definition covers both certain assent (taṣdīq) and conception (taṣawwur). Consider why conception, which seems to have no opposite, can still be included.
The commentator explains that the opposite is considered for the distinction, not for the knowledge itself.
3
Reflect on the significance
Why is this definition important? It sets the scope of knowledge, including both conceptual understanding and judgment. It also distinguishes knowledge from mere opinion or doubt.
This definition will be used to discuss the nature of divine knowledge later.

المرصد الثاني - في تعريف مطلق العلم المتفرع على الصفة إنما هو له لا للصفة ولا شك أن تمييزه إنما هو لشيء تتعلق به تلك الصفة والتمييز، وذلك الشيء هو الذي لا يحتمل النقيض، وهذا الحد يتناول التصديق اليقيني وهو ظاهر والتصور أيضاً، إذ لا نقيض له لأن المتناقضين هما المتصورة وفي التصديق النفي، أو الإثبات والمتعلق الطرفان كذا أفاده الشارح في حواشي شرح مختصر الأصول. وله : إذ لا نقيض (له أي لنميزه بناء على أن التصور والتصديق اليقيني عبارتان عما يوجب الصورة، والنفي والإثبات لكن ظاهر ،قوله ولا مانع بين التصورات فإن مفهومي الإنسان إلخ يأباه، فيحتاج إلى العناية في مواضع عديدة فالأظهر أن يؤول قوله وهذا الحد يتناول بمعنى يتناول ما يوجبهما، ويحتمل التصديق والتصور على المعنى المتعارف أعني الحكم والصورة. قوله : ( والتصور أيضاً إذ لا نقيض له أي لتمييزه على حذف المضاف إذ المعتبر في العلم عدم احتمال نقيض التمييز ثم التمييز في التصور نفس الصورة، والمتعلق الماهية المتصورة وفي التصديقات الإثبات أو النفي، والمتعلق الطرفان ولا يخفى أن الأولى لا نقيض لها، والأخيرين كل منهما نقيض الآخر كذا حققه الشارح في التصور نفس تلك الصورة، بل صفة موجبة لها وهذا مخالف لما تقرر عندهم على أنا لا نسلم أن لنا صفة موجبة توجب الإثبات والنفي والصورة العقلية بل ليس لنا في الواقع إلا أحدها، فالصواب أن يراد بالصفة نفس الصورة العقلية وبالتمييز المعنى المصدري، ويكون المعنى لا يحتمل متعلق ذلك التمييز نقيض تلك الصفة، إذ لا يحتمل متعلق التمييز نقيض نفسه بالقياس إلى المدرك، فمتعلق التمييز في التصور أعني المتصور لا نقيض له فلا وأما التصديق لأنه إذا كان مطابقاً جازماً لم يحتمل بالقياس إليه، وإذا فات شيء من الصفات احتمله والشارح المحقق إنما لم يحمل التعريف على هذين الوجهين اتباعاً لما ذكره المصنف في شرح الأصول أن متعلق The second observation post: On the definition of absolute knowledge. What is derived from the attribute belongs to it, not to the attribute, and there is no doubt that its distinction is only for something to which that attribute and the distinction are attached, and that thing is that which does not admit the opposite. This definition covers certain assent, which is apparent, and also conception, because it has no opposite, since the two contradictories are the conceived things, while in assent there is negation or affirmation, and the two relata are the two terms. Thus the commentator (al-Sharih) stated in his glosses on Sharh Mukhtasar al-Usul. And he said: 'Since there is no opposite for it' i.e., for its distinction, based on the fact that conception and certain assent are expressions for what necessitates the form and negation/affirmation, but his statement 'and there is no mutual prevention between conceptions, for the concepts of 'human' etc.' opposes it, so careful treatment is needed in many places. The most apparent is to interpret his words 'and this definition covers' as meaning 'covers what necessitates them', and it includes assent and conception according to the common meaning, i.e., judgment and form. His words: 'and conception also, since it has no opposite', i.e., for its distinction, by deleting the genitive construction, because what matters in knowledge is the non-admission of the opposite of the distinction, then the distinction in conception is the image itself, and the relatum is the conceived essence, while in assents it is affirmation or negation, and the two relata are the two terms. It is not hidden that the first has no opposite, and the latter two each are the opposite of the other. Thus the commentator verified in his glosses on Sharh al-Adud, so the necessary consequence that conception would not be knowledge but rather a distinction supervening on an attribute that is knowledge does not follow, and similarly for assent. But it follows that assent would not be the affirmation and negation themselves, but rather an attribute necessitating them, and similarly conception would not be that image itself, but rather an attribute necessitating it, and this contradicts what is established among them, especially since we do not concede that we have an attribute that necessitates affirmation, negation, and the mental image; rather in reality we have only one of them. So the correct view is that by 'attribute' is meant the mental image itself, and by 'distinction' the verbal noun meaning, and the meaning becomes: the relatum of that distinction does not admit the opposite of that attribute, since the relatum of the distinction does not admit the opposite of itself relative to the perceiver. Thus the relatum of the distinction in conception, i.e., the conceived thing, has no opposite, so it does not admit it at all; and the relatum of assent, i.e., the occurrence of the relation in reality, has an opposite, which is its non-occurrence. So each of conception and assent is an attribute that necessitates disclosure and clarification, whose relatum does not admit its opposite relative to the perceiver. As for conception, it is apparent; as for assent, because if it is certain and conforming, it does not admit relative to it, and if some attributes are missing, it admits it. And the verifying commentator (al-Sharih al-Muhaqqiq) did not apply the definition to these two ways only in following what the author mentioned in Sharh al-Usul: that the relatum of distinction in assent is the two terms, and that what is considered is the opposite of this distinction.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage defines absolute knowledge (al-ʿilm al-muṭlaq) and clarifies its scope. It begins by stating that knowledge is a quality (ṣifa) that brings about a distinction (tamyīz) for something that does not admit its opposite (naqīḍ). The author then claims this definition covers both certain assent (al-taṣdīq al-yaqīnī) and conception (al-taṣawwur). To defend this, he explains that conception has no opposite because opposites are only conceived in assent via negation or affirmation; the object of conception is a simple essence without contrast. The commentator (al-Shāriḥ) addresses an objection: if conception has no opposite, it would be a mere distinction rather than knowledge. He responds that the definition considers the opposite of the distinction, not of the knowledge itself. He then explores a further issue: if knowledge is a quality causing the image, then assent and conception are not the image/judgment themselves but qualities causing them, contradicting common usage. The correct interpretation is that 'attribute' refers to the mental image itself, and 'distinction' is the verbal noun, so the definition means the relatum of that distinction does not admit the opposite of that attribute. For conception the relatum has no opposite; for assent its relatum (the occurrence of the relation) does. Thus both remain valid as knowledge. The commentator notes that the author of the matn did not adopt this refined interpretation because he followed the earlier view that the relatum of distinction in assent is the two terms (the subject and predicate).

Key Terms
العلم
التمييز
النقيض
التصديق
التصور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Conception is not knowledge because it has no opposite to exclude.
Correction: The definition considers the opposite of the distinction, not of the knowledge itself. Conception has no opposite for its object, so it satisfies the definition.

Checkpoint
According to the definition in this passage, what is the thing that does not admit the opposite?
A) The attribute
B) The distinction
C) The thing to which the attribute and distinction are attached
D) The knowledge itself

Key Takeaway
The definition of knowledge as that whose object does not admit its opposite encompasses both conception and assent, with conception included because its object has no opposite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Iji defines knowledge as that whose object does not admit its opposite. Consider one of your core Islamic beliefs—for example, that Allah is One. Does your conviction admit any possibility of its opposite? If so, what steps will you take today to move from probable belief to certain knowledge that excludes any opposite?
?
The definition highlights 'distinction' (tamyīz) as central to knowledge. In your daily life, when you encounter claims about religion, do you actively distinguish truth from falsehood using the criteria of certainty? Where have you been lazy in applying this distinction, and what will you do to sharpen it starting today?
?
The commentator notes that in assent there are two relata—subject and predicate. When you say 'I believe in Allah,' are both the subject and predicate affirmed with the same certainty? Which part of that statement do you most struggle to hold without doubt, and what concrete action can you take to strengthen that assent?

Objection: All Conceptions Would Be Knowledge? Response by Error in Judgment
Refutes the objection that all conceptions are knowledge by distinguishing conception from judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why conceptions are never intrinsically false and why error always occurs in the judgment (ḥukm) that links a conception to an external object.

Core Idea
Error is not in the conception itself, but in the mind's judgment connecting it to reality.

This passage addresses a critical objection to the definition of knowledge: if conception has no contradictory, then even mistaken images would be knowledge. The response clarifies that conception is never non-conformant; error occurs only when the mind makes a judgment about that conception's correspondence to an external object. The example of seeing a stone from a distance and forming a human image illustrates this key distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the opening lines where the objection is raised and the immediate response: conception is not described by non-conformance at all.
The objection assumes that some conceptions are non-conformant, but the response denies that premise.
2
Study the stone-human example
Focus on the example: seeing a stone from afar and forming an image of a human. Identify what the conception is (the image of a human) and what the error is (the judgment that it corresponds to the stone).
3
Understand the distinction between conception and judgment
Note that the passage explicitly says error is only in the mind's judgment, not in the conception. Conceptions are like reports; they become true or false only when attached to a judgment about the external world.
This is the central doctrinal point.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this distinction matters: it preserves the definition of knowledge as true belief (or conception) while accounting for perceptual errors without compromising the intrinsic validity of conceptions.
The passage also mentions that the judgment can become a disposition (malaka) of the soul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

للإنسان، وعلم تصوري به والخطأ إنما هو في حكم العقل بأن هذه الصورة للشبح المرئي، فالتصورات بها أخبار والأخبار بعد العلم بها أوصاف وتعرض للاعتبارات الثلاثة في حواشي مختصر الأصول استيفاء للاعتبارات . قوله مجاز على التأويل) أي التأويل في مفهوم النقيضين بأن يراد بهما المتباعدان غاية التباعد سواء كانا متمانعين أولاً، أو التأويل بأن الحكم على الأطراف بالنقيض باعتبار الحكم المقارن لتصوراتها، وهو أن هذه بإدراك الأشياء على ما هي عليه واعلم أن ما ذكرناه حل لعبارة الشرح وأما تفصيل الكلام في التعريف والإيرادات عليه والأجوبة عنها فمذكور في حواشينا على الحواشي الخيالية فإن شئت فارجع إليه . المشتملة عليه موجبة سالبة المحمول فتنافي القضيتين كذباً ظاهر، وإن أخذ بمعنى العدول كما هو الظاهر، ينبغي أن يقيد بوجود الموضوع، وإلا فالموجبتان المذكورتان قد ترتفعان عند عدم الموضوع، ولو اقتصر على ذكر التنافي في الصدق لكان أظهر كما في حواشي العضد فتأمل. قوله : ( فإنا إذا رأينا من بعيد شبحاً قيل : يرد عليه أنه فرق بين العلم بالوجه والعلم بالشيء من ذلك الوجه، فالمتصور في المثال المذكور هو الشبح والصورة الذهنية It is not said: Accordingly, all conceptions are knowledge despite some of them being non-conformant. For we say: Conception is not described by non-conformance at all. For if we see from a distance a phantom that is, for example, a stone, and from it a form of a human arises in our minds, that form is a form of a human, and it is conceptual knowledge of it, and error is only in the mind's judgment that this form belongs to the visible phantom. So conceptions are reports and reports after knowledge of them are attributes. And he presents three considerations in the glosses on Mukhtasar al-Uṣūl for the sake of completeness. His saying: (metaphorical according to interpretation) i.e., interpretation in the concept of the two contradictories: either that they are intended as extremely distant, whether they are mutually exclusive or not, or the interpretation that the judgment about the parts as contradictory is by virtue of the judgment accompanying their conceptions, namely that this image is for that thing. The first is more appropriate, and the second is held by al-Fāḍil al-Abharī. His saying: (Accordingly) i.e., if conceptual concepts have no contradictory, then all conceptions are what necessitate forms as knowledges despite some forms being non-conformant, as when we conceive a thing in a way that is not its way. His saying: (For if we see etc.) If sense perception is included in knowledge, it is an example; otherwise it is an analogy. His saying: (It is only in the judgment of the mind, and this judgment has become a disposition of the soul due to its habit of perceiving things as they are) And know that what we have mentioned is a solution for the wording of the commentary. As for the detailed discussion of the definition, the objections against it, and the answers to them, it is mentioned in our glosses on the Khayālī glosses, so refer to it if you wish. [The proposition] composed of an affirmative and a negative predicate, so the contradiction of the two propositions in falsehood is obvious. And if taken in the sense of modification, as is apparent, it should be restricted to the existence of the subject, otherwise the two affirmative propositions mentioned may both be absent when the subject does not exist. And if he had restricted the mention of contradiction to truth, it would have been more apparent as in the glosses of al-ʿAḍud, so reflect. His saying: (For if we see a phantom from a distance) It is said: an objection is raised against him that there is a difference between knowledge by way of a face and knowledge of a thing from that face. So what is conceived in the mentioned example is the phantom, and the mental form is an instrument for observing it. And it is not hidden from you that it goes back to what the commentator mentioned. For if from a stone a form of a human arises in the mind, the human form is a mirror for observing individual humans in reality, and there is no error in that. Error is only in the mind's judgment that that form is an instrument for observing that visible phantom. For this judgment, and the judgment that what arises in the mind is a form of a human, are like two concomitants of this conception. Hence it is said: The dispute about the conception necessitating assent is carried on other than these two. And that conformity is also among the attributes of judgment, and what is described by it here is the latter judgment, even if the first is apparently refutable by the fact that the mentioned judgment has become a disposition of the soul, not that it is from the conception's necessitation of assent. And know that just as conception is not truly described by non-conformance, nor
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage refutes the objection that if conception (taṣawwur) has no contradictory, then all conceptions would be knowledge even if they are non-conformant. The response is that conception is never described as non-conformant; error is only in the judgment (ḥukm) of the mind that associates a conception with an external object. The example of seeing a stone from a distance and forming an image of a human illustrates this: the image is a true conception of 'human', but the error lies in the judgment that this image corresponds to the visible stone. The passage then discusses the metaphorical interpretation of 'contradictory' applied to propositional parts, noting two interpretations and preferring the one by extreme distance. Further commentary explains that the mental form of a human is a mirror for observing actual humans, and the error is only in the judgment that this form applies to the stone seen. This judgment becomes a habit (malaka) of the soul. The author concludes by referring to his own glosses for a detailed discussion and notes that the dispute about conception necessitating assent does not apply here, and that conformity is a property of judgment, not conception.

Misconception: Some students of logic think that conceptions can be false or non-conformant to reality.
Correction: The passage clarifies that conception itself is never non-conformant; error occurs only in the judgment that links the conception to an external object.

C) Because the image is a true knowledge of the stone.

Key Takeaway
Conception itself is never false; falsehood arises from the judgment associating a conception with an external object.

Which of your current certainties might actually be a judgment error disguised as truth? What step will you take today to test one of your habitual judgments against the source?

Objection from Habitual Sciences to the Definition
Objection against the definition using habitual sciences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why habitual sciences are considered to admit the possibility of contradiction, and how this objection challenges the definition of knowledge as that which excludes contradiction.

Core Idea
Habitual sciences seem certain but logically allow the opposite because God can break custom.

This lesson unpacks a key objection against a specific definition of knowledge. The objection argues that habitual sciences—things we know from repeated experience—actually permit the possibility of their opposite, due to God's unrestricted power. This challenges whether such sciences fit the definition, which requires exclusion of contradiction. Let's examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection statement
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the objection is introduced against the 'chosen definition'.
The definition in question is not repeated here, but it is one that requires knowledge to not admit contradiction.
2
Identify the example given
The objection uses the example of knowing a mountain has not turned into gold. This is a habitual science. Ask yourself: why does this example challenge the definition?
3
Understand the reasoning behind the possibility
The passage explains that habitual sciences admit contradiction because God's power is all-encompassing and atoms are equivalent in receiving opposite qualities. This makes the opposite logically possible, even if not actual.
This is a key metaphysical assumption: atoms are neutral to qualities.
4
Connect the objection to the definition
If habitual sciences admit possibility of contradiction, they would be excluded from the definition (since the definition requires no possible contradiction). Yet they are clearly knowledge. Thus the definition is too narrow.
This is the force of the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و أورد على الحد المختار العلوم العادية و هي العلوم المستندة إلى العادة، كعلمنا مثلا بأن الجبل الذي رأيناه فيما مضى لم ينقلب الآن ذهباً فإنها تحتمل النقيض فتخرج عن الحد مع كونها من أفراد المحدود. و إنما كانت محتملة له لجواز خرق العادة، فنقول مثلا في المثال المذكور أن شمول قدرة المختار مع استواء الجواهر الأفراد في قبول الصفات المتقابلة كالذهبية و الحجرية إذا كانت متناسبة متجانسة في الأجسام كما ذهب إليه بعضهم يوجب ذلك الاحتمال
And it is objected against the chosen definition by the habitual sciences—these are the sciences based on habit, like our knowledge, for example, that the mountain which we saw in the past has not now turned into gold. For they admit the possibility of the opposite, thus they would be excluded from the definition despite being instances of the defined. They are only capable of this because of the possibility of breaking custom. For we say, for example, in the mentioned example, that the inclusiveness of the power of the Choser (God) together with the equivalence of individual atoms in accepting opposite qualities such as gold-ness and stone-ness, if they are proportionally homogeneous in bodies as some have held, necessitates that possibility.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the definition of knowledge as something that does not admit contradiction. The objection cites habitual sciences ('ulūm 'ādiyya) — sciences that arise from custom, such as our knowledge that a mountain we saw yesterday has not turned into gold today. These sciences seem to admit the possibility of their opposite because it is possible for God to break the usual course of events (khārq al-'āda). Therefore, according to the definition, habitual sciences would be excluded from knowledge, yet they are clearly instances of knowledge. The author elaborates the objection: the power of the Chosen (God) is all-encompassing, and atomic substances are equivalent in their capacity to receive opposite qualities like gold-ness and stone-ness, provided the bodies are homogeneous — as some philosophers hold. This necessitates the possibility of the opposite, thus the habitual sciences are not certain.

خرق العادة
احتمال النقيض

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Habitual sciences are absolutely certain and do not admit any possibility of their opposite.
Correction: The objection shows that because of God's power and atomic equivalence, habitual sciences are based on custom and logically allow the opposite, even if it never occurs.

Checkpoint

B) Because God's power is inclusive and atoms are equivalent
C) Because they are not real sciences
D) Because they rely on analogy

Key Takeaway
The objection from habitual sciences argues that because God can break custom and atoms are receptive to opposites, habitual knowledge admits possible contradiction, challenging any definition of knowledge that excludes such possibility.

Possibility and Substitution in Habitual Knowledge
Replies that actual breaking of custom, not mere possibility, negates knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the possibility of different essences does not negate habitual knowledge, because judgments can be based on a common denominator and the opposite occurs only as substitution.

Core Idea
Possibility of the opposite is only a substitution, not a real threat to certainty.

In this passage, the author addresses a subtle objection: if atoms have different essences, doesn't that introduce real possibility? The reply shows that by taking a common denominator as the subject, the judgment avoids particularity, and any opposite is merely a substitution, not a real conflict. This preserves the certainty of habitual knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once without analyzing. Notice the structure: an objection is raised, then answered.
The text is dense; focus on the flow of reasoning.
2
Identify the objection being answered
The objection claims that if atoms have different essences, then the possibility of the opposite is real, which would undermine habitual knowledge. Find where this is stated.
The phrase 'إذا قيل أنها متخالفة الماهية' signals the objection.
3
Understand the common denominator concept
The author uses 'common denominator' (قدر مشترك) like 'the occupant of that place' to make the judgment independent of particular essences. See how this avoids the problem.
The judgment is not about the mountain's particularity.
4
Grasp the substitution point
The author concludes that the opposite possibility is only a substitution (إبدال), not a real conflict. This means habitual knowledge remains certain.
The title as a common denominator is undisputed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و إذا قيل أنها متخالفة الماهية و ما يوجب ذلك الاحتمال، فإنا نأخذ الموضوع ما هو قدر مشترك بينهما كالشاغل للمكان الفلاني من غير ملاحظة خصوصية كونه جبلاً فلا يكون الحكم وارداً على خصوصية لا الجبل، حتى يصح الحكم عليه بجواز كونه ذهباً، قيل : المتصف بالحجرية في نفس الأمر هو مجموع جواهر مخصوصة مسماة بالجبل لا مفهوم الشاغل الذي جعل عنواناً، و آلة للحكم فعلى تقدير تخالف الجواهر لم يحتمل النقيض في نفس الأمر، وهو ظاهر. وأما الحاكم فالظاهر أنه أراد بالشاغل الفلاني العهد الخارجي فإن اعتقد تخالفها بالحقيقة فلا يحتمله عنده أيضاً وإلا احتمله. لكن لو أخذ الموضوع خصوصية الجبل، لكان الأمر كذلك فلا فرق بين أخذ الموضوع معيناً و بين أخذ مشتركا في أن وصف الحجرية و الذهبية لا يردان على موضوع واحد، و الاحتمال للنقيض على تقدير التخالف لا يكون إلا على وجه الإبدال. غاية ما في الباب أن العنوان على تقدير كونه قدراً مشتركاً واحداً لا نزاع فيه.
And if it is said that they (the atoms) are of different essences, and that which necessitates that possibility, we take the subject as a common denominator between them, like 'the occupant of that place' without observing the particularity of its being a mountain, so the judgment is not applied to a particularity, i.e., the mountain, so that it would be valid to judge upon it the possibility of its being gold. It is said: the thing described by stone-ness in actual fact is the collection of particular atoms named 'mountain', not the concept of occupant which is made a title and instrument for judgment. So on the assumption of the difference of the atoms, it does not admit the opposite in actual fact, and that is apparent. As for the judge, it seems that he meant by 'the occupant of that place' the external particular. So if he believes in their real difference, then it does not admit the opposite for him either; otherwise, it admits it. However, if the subject is taken as the particularity of the mountain, the matter would be the same. There is no difference between taking the subject as a particular and taking it as a common denominator in that the descriptions of stone-ness and gold-ness are not applied to one subject. The possibility of the opposite on the assumption of difference only occurs in the manner of substitution. The point of the discussion is that the title, on the assumption of being a common denominator, is not disputed.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is the answer to the objection about habitual sciences. The author considers a counter-argument: if the atoms are of different essences (not homogeneous), then the possibility mentioned earlier does not arise. To address this, the author explains that we can take the subject as a common denominator (قدر مشترك) such as 'the occupant of that place' without specifying that it is a mountain. Then the judgment is not about the mountain's particularity, and the possibility of it being gold becomes a judgment about that common subject. However, it is remarked that the actual thing described by stone-ness is the collection of specific atoms called 'mountain', not the concept of occupant. On the assumption of different essences, the opposite is not actually possible. The judge here likely meant by 'occupant' the external particular. If he believes in real difference, for him the opposite is not possible; otherwise it is. Ultimately, whether the subject is particular or common, the descriptions of stone-ness and gold-ness cannot be applied to the same subject; the possibility of the opposite on the assumption of difference is only a substitution (إبدال). The key point is that the title as a common denominator is not disputed. This shows that habitual knowledge can be considered certain even if a conceptual possibility exists.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One may think that a common denominator introduces uncertainty because it abstracts from particularity.
Correction: The author shows that the common denominator does not affect the actual state of affairs; the opposite possibility is only a substitution, preserving certainty.

Checkpoint

A) Ignoring the real essence of the mountain
B) Using a general description like 'the occupant of that place' to avoid the problem of particularity
C) Assuming all atoms are the same
D) Denying the possibility of breaking custom

Key Takeaway
Even if atoms have different essences, habitual knowledge remains certain because the possibility of the opposite is merely a substitution, not a real conflict with the actual state.

Customary Sciences and Two Kinds of Probability
Reply to objection against definition using two probabilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection from customary sciences is answered by distinguishing essential possibility from probability of error.

Core Idea
Customary knowledge is certain despite metaphysical possibility of miracles.

Before we can accept a definition of knowledge, we must test it against counterexamples. Customary sciences—like knowing a mountain remains gold—appear to admit the opposite, which would violate the definition. The author responds by clarifying two distinct meanings of 'probability'.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read carefully the objection that customary sciences admit the opposite, then the answer begins with 'والجواب'. Notice how the answer distinguishes two probabilities.
The answer is nuanced; pay attention to the shift from the first probability to the second.
2
Distinguish the two probabilities
Identify the first probability as essential possibility (imkan dhati) and the second as the probability of error in the judgment itself. Compare their definitions in the text.
The first pertains to the object's nature; the second pertains to the reliability of the judgment.
3
Apply the distinction to the objection
Understand that the definition only denies the second probability, which is absent in customary knowledge. This resolves the objection without excluding customary sciences.
Customary knowledge is certain because the opposite is not actually judged.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأورد على الحد المختار العلوم العادية وهي العلوم المستندة إلى العادة، كعلمنا مثلا بأن الجبل الذي رأيناه فيما مضى لم ينقلب الآن ذهباً فإنها تحتمل النقيض، فتخرج عن الحد مع كونها من أفراد المحدود، وإنما كانت محتملة له لجواز خرق العادة. والجواب أن يقال: احتمال العاديات للنقيض بمعنى أنه لو فرض نقيضها واقعاً بدلها لم يلزم منه محال لذاته لأن تلك الأمور العادية ممكنة في ذواتها والممكن لا يستلزم بشيء من طرفيه محالاً لذاته غير احتمال متعلق التميز الواقع فيه أي في العلم العادي للنقيض. وذلك لأن الاحتمال الأول راجع إلى الإمكان الذاتي الثابت للممكنات في حد ذواتها، والاحتمال الثاني: هو أن يكون متعلق التمييز محتملاً لأن يحكم فيه المميز بنقيضه في الحال كما في الظن أو في المآل كما في الجهل المركب والتقليد، ومنشؤه ضعف ذلك التمييز إما لعدم الجزم أو لعدم المطابقة أو لعدم استناده إلى موجب، وهذا الاحتمال الثاني المغاير للأول هو المراد من الاحتمال المذكور في التعريف، وهو الذي ورد عليه النفي فيه، وإنه ممنوع ثبوته في العلوم العادية.
And an objection was raised against the chosen definition: the customary sciences — which are the sciences based on habit, such as our knowledge, for example, that the mountain we saw in the past has not now turned into gold — for they admit the opposite, so they would be excluded from the definition despite being individuals of the defined. And the answer is to say: The probability of the opposite in customary matters means that if its opposite were assumed to occur in its place, no impossibility would follow from it in itself, because those customary things are possible in their essences, and the possible does not entail an impossibility in itself from any of its two sides — not the probability of the belonger of the distinction occurring in it, i.e., in customary knowledge, of the opposite. That is because the first probability reduces to the essential possibility established for possibles in the limit of their essences, and the second probability is that the belonger of the distinction is likely that the distinguisher judges its opposite in the present, as in opinion, or in the future, as in compound ignorance and imitation, and its source is the weakness of that distinction, either due to lack of certainty, or lack of correspondence, or lack of being based on a necessitator. And this second probability, different from the first, is what is intended by the probability mentioned in the definition, and it is what the negation applies to in it, and its existence in the customary sciences is denied.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the definition of knowledge and then answers it. The objector argues that customary sciences (e.g., knowing a mountain hasn't turned to gold) are not certain because miracles could happen, so they admit the opposite (ihtimal al-naqid), and thus would be excluded from the definition even though they are valid knowledge. The answer distinguishes two kinds of probability. The first is essential possibility (imkan dhati): the object itself is metaphysically capable of being otherwise; this is not what the definition denies. The second is the probability that the judgment itself could be wrong in the present or future, as in opinion, compound ignorance, or imitation — this stems from a weakness in the distinction (tamyiz). The definition's denial of 'probability of the opposite' refers only to this second kind, and it is not present in customary sciences because the mind is certain that the opposite is not actual, even if it is possible in itself.

Key Terms
العلوم العادية
احتمال النقيض
الإمكان الذاتي
التمييز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Customary knowledge is uncertain because miracles are possible.
Correction: Metaphysical possibility does not undermine the certainty of the judgment itself; the denial in the definition refers only to the probability of error in the judgment.

Checkpoint
What is the second kind of probability that the definition of knowledge denies?
A) Essential possibility (al-imkan al-dhati)
B) The possibility that the judgment itself could be mistaken
C) The possibility of a miracle occurring in the future
D) The possibility that the object might not exist

Key Takeaway
The definition of knowledge denies only the probability of error in the judgment, not the metaphysical possibility of the opposite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often feel certain about your faith, but sometimes a thought creeps in: 'What if it's all a possibility that could be otherwise?' How does this lesson's distinction between essential possibility and probability of error change your reaction to that thought? Will you now dismiss it as irrelevant to your certainty?
?
This lesson says the second probability arises from weakness in your distinction (tamyiz). Where in your daily life do you allow doubt because your judgment lacks certainty or a sure foundation? What one action will you take today to strengthen that distinction?
?
The objector thought customary knowledge was uncertain because miracles are possible. Do you sometimes treat your knowledge of Allah and His attributes as if it could be undermined by mere possibility? After this lesson, how will you train your mind to separate essential possibility from actual uncertainty? Make a concrete commitment to remind yourself of this when doubt arises.

Subject as a Common Notion in Customary Knowledge
Explains that the subject in customary knowledge is taken as a common notion to allow for the opposite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how customary knowledge takes the subject as a common notion to preserve the possibility of opposite judgments.

Core Idea
A common subject allows contradictory attributes without contradiction.

In this passage, the commentator addresses a potential flaw in the definition of kalam: if a subject's specific essence prevents change, then judgments on it cannot admit opposites. He shows that in customary knowledge, we do not take the specific essence but a common notion, like 'the occupant of that place', which can receive different attributes at different times.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem
Read the objection: if the mountain's essence is different from gold's essence, then no single subject can have both rockness and goldness, so the judgment on the mountain does not admit its opposite.
2
See the solution
The rebuttal introduces a common notion (qadr mushtarak) as the subject, such as 'the occupant of that place'. This common notion can be described by both attributes at different times without contradiction.
3
Apply to the definition of kalam
Reflect on how this refinement ensures that customary knowledge satisfies the definition's condition of admitting the opposite (ihtimal al-naqid).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (قلنا : نحن نعلم بالعادة إلخ ) يريد دفع ما يقال من أن ما تركب منه الجبل إذا كان مخالفاً في الحقيقة لا تركب منه الذهب، لم يكن هناك موضوع معين يصح أن يتوارد عليه هذان الوصفان المتنافيان فليس الحكم على الجبل بأحدهما محتملا لنقيضه نعم يمكن أن يعدم الجبل، ويوجد الذهب مكانه فيختلف الموضوع، فلا تنافي بين الحكمين فلا احتمال للنقيض، ووجه الدفع أنا نأخذ الموضوع ما هو قدر مشترك بينهما كالشاغل للمكان الفلاني.
His statement: 'We know by habit, etc.' intends to rebut what is said: that if that from which the mountain is composed is actually different in reality, gold is not composed from it, there would be no definite subject upon which these two contradictory attributes can both be predicated, so the judgment about the mountain by one of them would not be probable of its opposite; indeed, it is possible that the mountain be annihilated and gold be brought into existence in its place, so the subject differs, so there is no contradiction between the two judgments, so there is no probability of the opposite. And the way of rebuttal is that we take the subject as a common notion between the two, such as the occupant of that place.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential problem: if the mountain's specific essence prevents it from being gold, then there is no single subject that can receive both attributes, so the judgment that it is rock does not admit the opposite. The answer is that we take the subject not as the specific essence of the mountain but as a common notion (qadr mushtarak), such as 'the occupant of that particular place' (al-shaghal li-makan kadhālik). This common notion can be described by both rockness and goldness at different times, preserving the possibility of the opposite without contradiction. This refinement ensures that customary knowledge still meets the definition's condition.

Key Terms
قدر مشترك
الموضوع
الشاغل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The subject of a judgment must be a specific essence.
Correction: In customary knowledge, the subject can be a common notion that allows for change, such as 'the occupant of that place'.

Checkpoint
What is taken as the subject in customary knowledge to allow for the possibility of the opposite?
A) The specific essence of the mountain
B) A common notion like 'the occupant of that place'
C) The attribute of rockness itself
D) The judgment of the distinguisher

Key Takeaway
Customary knowledge preserves the possibility of opposite judgments by taking the subject as a common notion rather than a specific essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you judge something as unchangeable, do you base that judgment on its specific essence or on a common notion that could allow for change? How would your certainty hold up if you realized the subject was only a placeholder?
?
Is there a belief you hold about yourself or others that you treat as if it belongs to the essence, but in reality it may only be a common notion? Would you be willing to admit the possibility of its opposite?
?
Think of a time you insisted someone was 'always' a certain way. Did you assume their essence was fixed? Can you now see them as a 'common notion' subject, capable of change, and what would that demand from your heart?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
5 min
Two Kinds of Probability in Knowledge
Distinguishing essential possibility from probability of judgment in defining knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between essential possibility and probability of judgment, and why only the latter is negated in the definition of knowledge.

Core Idea
Knowledge's certainty does not deny essential possibility, only probability of judgment.

This passage addresses a nuanced objection about the definition of knowledge. The author explains that not all probability is alike: there is an essential possibility inherent in all created things, and a probability related to the weakness of a knower's judgment. The definition's negation applies only to the second, preserving the certainty of sense-based knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Pay attention to the parentheses, which indicate the author's interjections.
The passage is a response to an implicit objection about customary knowledge.
2
Identify the two probabilities
The author explicitly names two types: the first is essential possibility (الإمكان الذاتي) for all possible things; the second is probability related to the distinction (التمييز) and judgment. Notice their different implications.
The second probability can lead to error, unlike the first.
3
Understand the objection-answer structure
The passage begins with 'والجواب' (the answer), indicating it is responding to an objection. Follow how the author first states the answer, then clarifies the distinction, and finally concludes which probability is negated.
This structure is common in kalam texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( والجواب ) أن يقال : ( احتمال العاديات للنقيض بمعنى) أنه (لو فرض نقيضها ) واقعاً بدلها (لم يلزم منه ) أي من ذلك النقيض محال لذاته لأن تلك الأمور العادية ممكنة في ذواتها والممكن لا يستلزم بشيء من طرفيه محالاً لذاته (غير احتمال) متعلق (التميز الواقع فيه أي في العلم العادي للنقيض) وذلك لأن الاحتمال الأول راجع إلى الإمكان الذاتي الثابت للممكنات في حد ذواتها كما بيناه، والاحتمال الثاني : هو أن يكون متعلق التمييز محتملاً لأن يحكم فيه المميز بنقيضه في الحال كما في الظن أو في المآل كما في الجهل المركب، والتقليد ومنشؤه ضعف ذلك التمييز إما لعدم الجزم أو لعدم المطابقة، أو لعدم استناده إلى موجب (وهذا الاحتمال الثاني المغاير للأول (هو المراد من الاحتمال المذكور في التعريف، وهو الذي ورد عليه النفي فيه ( وإنه ممنوع) ثبوته في العلوم العادية كما في العلوم المستندة إلى الحس وثبوت الاحتمال الأول لا يقدح في شيء منهما
And the answer is to say: (The probability of customary matters for the opposite means) that (if its opposite were assumed) to occur in its place, (it would not necessitate) from it, i.e., from that opposite, an impossibility in itself, because those customary things are possible in their essences, and the possible does not necessitate from either of its extremes an impossibility in itself. (Other than the probability) related to (the distinction occurring in it, i.e., in customary knowledge, for the opposite). That is because the first probability refers to essential possibility established for possibles in the limit of their essences, as we explained. And the second probability is that the object of distinction is probable such that the distinguisher judges its opposite in the present, as in conjecture, or in the future, as in compounded ignorance and imitation; and its source is the weakness of that distinction, either due to lack of certainty, lack of correspondence, or lack of reliance on a cause. (And this second probability, different from the first, is what is meant by the probability mentioned in the definition, and it is the one to which negation applies. (And it is denied) its occurrence in customary sciences, as in the sciences based on sense perception; and the occurrence of the first probability does not harm either of them.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a definitional explanation. The author begins by presenting the answer to an objection about the probability of opposites in customary matters. He distinguishes two kinds of probability: first, the essential possibility (الإمكان الذاتي) inherent in all possible things, which is always present; second, the probability related to the distinction (التمييز) made by the knower, which can be affected by weakness. He clarifies that the second probability is the one intended in the definition of knowledge, and it is this probability that is negated in customary sciences (like sense-based knowledge). The first probability does not undermine the certainty of such knowledge.

Key Terms
احتمال
إمكان ذاتي
تمييز
العلوم العادية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition of knowledge denies all forms of probability, including the possibility that things could be otherwise.
Correction: The definition only denies the probability of a wrong judgment (probability of distinction), not the essential possibility that the known fact could have been different.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the two probabilities is the one meant in the definition of knowledge?
A) The first probability (essential possibility)
B) The second probability (probability of judgment)
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
In defining knowledge, only the probability of a wrong judgment is negated, not the essential possibility of the opposite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your certainty about daily matters—like that the sun will rise tomorrow—do you confuse essential possibility with probability of judgment? How would your reliance on such knowledge change if you admitted its essential contingency while still affirming its certainty as a judgment?
?
The passage ties probability of judgment to weakness in distinction—lack of certainty, lack of correspondence, or lack of a proper basis. Which of these three weaknesses do you find most often in your own 'certain' beliefs about religious matters? What would it cost you to honestly examine that?
?
If you discovered that one of your deeply held beliefs was based on a probability of judgment you had not considered, would you be willing to re-evaluate it? What fear prevents you from doing so?
